Proving the Vision of Allah by Division of Meanings
A proof by division showing the verse means literal seeing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ash'ari uses the method of division of meanings (sabr wa taqsim) to prove that the verse 'Looking at their Lord' refers to literal vision of Allah in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
Context determines the meaning of 'nazar' — and here it means seeing Allah.

Imam Ash'ari addresses a key dispute with the Mu'tazila over the beatific vision. He employs a rational method called 'division of meanings' to show that the only possible interpretation of the Quranic phrase 'looking at their Lord' is actual sight with the eyes. In this passage, he systematically eliminates alternative meanings and provides linguistic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four possible meanings
Read the passage and list the four meanings Ash'ari gives for 'nazar' (looking).
These are: reflection, waiting, compassion, and seeing.
2
Analyze the elimination of reflection and waiting
Note why reflection is impossible in the Hereafter and why waiting contradicts the nature of Paradise and the grammar of the verse.
Waiting is also linguistically incompatible with the preposition 'ila'.
3
Examine the elimination of compassion
Consider why showing compassion to Allah is not fitting, especially in Paradise.
4
Understand the linguistic refutation
See how Ash'ari uses Arabic usage (Quran and poetry) to prove that 'nazara ila' means seeing, not waiting.
This refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation.
5
Conclude the proof
The fourth meaning alone remains valid, establishing that the verse proves the beatific vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث
الكلام في إثبات رؤية الله تعالى بالأبصار في الآخرة
قال الله تعالى: ﴿ وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ ﴾ يعني مشرقة، ﴿ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ﴾ [القيامة: ٢٢-٢٣] يعني رائية، وليس يخلو النظر من وجوه نحن ذاكروها:
١ - إما أن يكون الله عز وجل عنى نظر الاعتبار، كقوله تعالى: ﴿ أَفَلَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَى الْإِبِلِ كَيْفَ خُلِقَتْ ﴾ [الغاشية: ١٧].
٢ - أو يكون عنى نظر الانتظار كقوله تعالى: ﴿ مَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَّا صَيْحَةً وَاحِدَةً ﴾ [يس: ٤٩].
٣ - أو أن يكون عنى نظر التعطف، كقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا يَنظُرُ إِلَيْهِمْ يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ ﴾ [آل عمران: ٧٧]
٤ - أو يكون عنى نظر الرؤية.
فلا يجوز أن يكون الله عز وجل عنى نظر التفكير والاعتبار؛ لأن الآخرة ليست بدار اعتبار.
ولا يجوز أن يكون عنى نظر الانتظار؛ لأن النظر إذا ذكر مع ذكر الوجه، فمعناه نظر العينين اللتين في الوجه، كما إذا ذكر أهل اللسان نظر القلب فقالوا: انظر في هذا الأمر بقلبك، لم يكن معناه نظر العينين، ولذلك إذا ذكر النظر مع الوجه لم يكن معناه نظر الانتظار الذي بالقلب، وأيضاً فإن نظر الانتظار لا يكون في الجنة؛ لأن الانتظار معه تنغيص وتكدير، وأهل الجنة لهم ( مَا لَا عَيْنٌ رَأَتْ وَلَا أُذُنٌ سَمِعَتْ ) من العيش السليم والنعيم المقيم، وإذا كان هذا هكذا، لم يجز أن يكونوا منتظرين؛ لأنهم كلما خطر ببالهم شيء أتوا به مع خطوره ببالهم.
وإذا كان ذلك كذلك، فلا يجوز أن يتعطفوا على خالقهم وإذا فسدت الأقسام الثلاثة، صح القسم الرابع من أقسام النظر، وهو أن معنى قوله: ﴿ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ﴾ [القيامة: ٢٣] أنها رائية ترى ربها عز وجل.
ومما يبطل قول المعتزلة: أن الله عز وجل أراد بقوله: ﴿ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ﴾ نظر الانتظار، أنه قال: ﴿ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ﴾ ونظر الانتظار لا يكون مقروناً بقوله: «إلى»؛ لأنه لا يجوز عند العرب أن يقولوا في نظر الانتظار «إلى»، ألا ترى أن الله تعالى لما قال: ﴿ مَا يَنظُرُونَ إِلَّا صَيْحَةً وَاحِدَةً ﴾ [يس: ٤٩] لم يقل (إلى)؛ إذا كان معناه الانتظار. وقال عز وجل مخبراً عن بلقيس: ﴿ فَنَاظِرَةٌ بِمَ يَرْجِعُ الْمُرْسَلُونَ ﴾ [النمل: ٣٥] فلما أرادت الانتظار لم تقل (إلى). وقال امرؤ القيس:
فَإِنَّكُمَا إِنْ تَنْظُرَانِي سَاعَةً مِنَ الدَّهْرِ تَنفَعْنِي لَدَى أُمِّ جُنْدَبِ
Chapter Three: Speech on Establishing the Vision of Allah Almighty with the Eyes in the Hereafter. Allah Almighty said: {Faces on that day are bright} meaning radiant, {looking at their Lord} [Al-Qiyamah: 22-23] meaning seeing. And the word 'nazar' (looking) cannot be free from [one of] aspects which we will mention: 1 - Either Allah Almighty intended the look of reflection, as His saying: {Do they not look at the camels, how they are created?} [Al-Ghashiyah: 17]. 2 - Or He intended the look of waiting, as His saying: {They await not but a single blast} [Ya-Sin: 49]. 3 - Or He intended the look of compassion, as His saying: {And He will not look at them on the Day of Resurrection} [Aal-e-Imran: 77]. 4 - Or He intended the look of seeing. It is not permissible that Allah Almighty intended the look of reflection and consideration, because the Hereafter is not a place of reflection. And it is not permissible that He intended the look of waiting, because when 'nazar' is mentioned along with mention of the face, its meaning is the sight of the two eyes that are in the face, just as when the people of language mention the heart's 'nazar' and say: 'Look into this matter with your heart,' it does not mean the sight of the eyes. Therefore, when 'nazar' is mentioned with the face, its meaning is not the look of waiting which is with the heart. Also, the look of waiting does not exist in Paradise, because waiting involves vexation and trouble, and the people of Paradise have what no eye has seen, no ear has heard, of a sound life and permanent bliss. And if this is so, it is not possible that they are waiting, because whenever a thing occurs to their minds, they are brought it at the moment it occurs to them. And if that is so, it is not possible that they [i.e., the faces] show compassion to their Creator. And when the three divisions are invalid, the fourth division of 'nazar' becomes valid, which is that the meaning of His saying: {Looking at their Lord} [Al-Qiyamah: 23] is that they see, they see their Lord Almighty. And among what refutes the statement of the Mu'tazila that Allah Almighty intended by His saying: {Looking at their Lord} the look of waiting, is that He said: {Looking at their Lord} and the look of waiting is not accompanied by the word 'ila' (at); because it is not permissible according to the Arabs to say in the look of waiting 'ila'. Do you not see that Allah Almighty when He said: {They await not but a single blast} [Ya-Sin: 49] did not say 'ila', because its meaning was waiting. And Allah Almighty said, informing about Bilqis: {So I am watching with what the messengers will return} [An-Naml: 35]. When she intended waiting, she did not say 'ila'. And Imru' al-Qais said: 'For if you two wait for me an hour of time, it will benefit me before Umm Jundub.'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a proof by division (sabr wa taqsim) to establish that the verse 'Looking at their Lord' (Al-Qiyamah: 23) refers to literal vision (ru'ya) with the eyes in the Hereafter, against the Mu'tazila who interpret it as 'waiting' (intidhar).

The author first states that the word 'nazar' (looking) can have four possible meanings: (1) reflection (i'tibar), as in the camel verse; (2) waiting (intidhar), as in 'await but a single blast'; (3) compassion (ta'attuf), as in 'He will not look at them'; and (4) seeing (ru'ya) with the eyes.

He then eliminates the first three for this context. Reflection is impossible because the Hereafter is not a place for reflection—it is a realm of recompense, not deduction. Waiting is impossible for two reasons. First, when 'nazar' is paired with 'face' in Arabic, it always means sight of the eyes, not an inner mental waiting. Just as 'nazar' of the heart is distinguished from eye-sight, when the face is mentioned, it specifically points to ocular vision. Second, waiting involves anxiety and delay, which contradicts the bliss of Paradise, where inhabitants instantly receive whatever they desire. Compassion is also impossible because it would imply deficiency on the part of the Creator or the creatures, and the context of Paradise excludes it.

With three meanings invalid, the fourth remains: seeing with the eyes. This is the correct interpretation of 'looking at their Lord'.

The author then adds a specific linguistic refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that the verse means waiting. He points out that the Arabic preposition 'ila' (to/at) is not used with the verb 'nazara' when it means 'to wait'. The examples from the Qur'an (Ya-Sin 49 without 'ila', and the story of Bilqis who said 'fanaziratun' without 'ila') and the line of Imru' al-Qais all show that waiting is expressed without 'ila'. Since the verse uses 'ila ila rabbiha', it cannot mean waiting; it must mean actual seeing.

Key Terms

الرؤية
الاعتبار
الانتظار
التعطف
السبر والتقسيم
المعتزلة
إلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nazar' always means 'seeing' with the eyes.
Correction: Ash'ari demonstrates that 'nazar' can mean reflection, waiting, or compassion, and context determines the correct meaning.

Checkpoint
Why does Ash'ari eliminate the interpretation of 'waiting' (intidhar) for the verse 'looking at their Lord'?
A) Because waiting implies anxiety, which is absent in Paradise.
B) Because the word 'ila' is used, which is not used with waiting.
C) Because the face is mentioned, and waiting is a heart-action.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Using division of meanings, Imam Ash'ari proves that the verse 'Looking at their Lord' unequivocally refers to seeing Allah with the eyes in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ', do you genuinely long for that moment of seeing your Lord with your own eyes in Paradise? Or have you become so familiar with the idea that it no longer stirs your soul? Write down one concrete change you will make today to intensify that longing.
?
The proof here eliminates waiting from Paradise because waiting brings vexation. Think about your daily life: are you waiting for something from Allah with anxiety and impatience? Or do you trust Him so deeply that your waiting is peaceful? What one worry can you hand over to Him right now, replacing it with certainty that He will provide at the perfect time?
?
If you truly believe that the believers will see Allah in the Hereafter, how does that affect the way you live now? Are you preparing your heart to meet Him, or are you distracted by lesser pursuits? Commit to one specific action this week that will orient your life toward that vision.

6 min
The Apparent Meaning of 'Looking at Their Lord' and Refuting Reinterpretation
Al-Ash'ari argues for literal interpretation of vision verse and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's linguistic and rational arguments for the apparent meaning of the verse 'to their Lord looking' and his refutation of the Mu'tazila reinterpretation.

Core Idea
The Quran's apparent meaning is the default; removing it requires proof.

Al-Ash'ari defends the Sunni belief that believers will see Allah in the afterlife by analyzing the Quranic phrase 'to their Lord looking'. He argues that the wording and context require taking the verse literally, and he turns the Mu'tazila's own method against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall argument.
2
Understand the linguistic point about 'nazira'
Notice how Al-Ash'ari uses grammar to show that 'nazira' here means seeing, not waiting.
He contrasts with the verb for waiting which doesn't use 'ila' in the same way.
3
Examine the principle of apparent meaning
Al-Ash'ari establishes that the Quran is to be interpreted according to its apparent meaning unless there is a proof to change it.
He gives an example from prayer.
4
Follow the refutation of Mu'tazila
See how Al-Ash'ari uses a reductio ad absurdum to show the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila position.
If they can reinterpret one verse, others can reinterpret another in a similar way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلما أراد الانتظار لم يقل : « إلى » فلما قال سبحانه: ﴿ إلى ربها ناظرة ) علمنا أنه لم يرد الانتظار، وإنما أراد نظر الرؤية . ولما قرن الله النظر بذكر الوجه أراد نظر العينين اللتين في الوجه، كما قال: قد نَرَى تَقَلُّبَ وَجْهِكَ في السَّمَاءِ فَلَنُوَلِيَنَكَ قِبْلَةً تَرْضَهَا) [البقرة : ١٤٤]، فذكر الوجه، وإنما أراد تَقَلَّبَ عينيه نحو السماء ينتظر نزول الملك عليه بصرف الله لـه عـن قبلة بيت المقدس إلى الكعبة . فإن قال قائل : لم لا تقولون : إن قوله : إلَى رَيْهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ( إنما أراد إلى ثواب ربها ناظرة ؟ قيل له ثواب الله عز وجل غيره تعالى والله سبحانه وتعالى قال: إلى ربها نَاظِرَةٌ ولم يقل : إلى غيره ناظرة والقرآن العزيز على ظاهره، وليس لنا أن نزيله عن ظاهره إلا بحجة، وإلا فهو على ظاهره . ألا ترى أن الله عز وجل لما قال : صَلَّوا لي واعبدوني، لم يجز أن يقول قائل : إنه أراد غيره ويزيل الكلام عن ظاهره ؛ فلذلك لما قال: ﴿ إِلَى رَيْهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ) لم يجز لنا أن نزيل القرآن عن ظاهره بغير حجة . ثم يقال للمعتزلة : إن جاز لكم أن تزعموا أن قول الله عز وجل: وإلى ريها ناظرة إنما أراد به أنها إلى غيره ناظرة ، فَلِمَ لا جاز لغيركم أن يقول: إن قول الله عز وجل: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ﴾ [الأنعام: ۱۰۳] أراد بها لا تدرك غيره، ولم يرد أنها لا تدركه؟ وهذا ما لا يقدرون على الفرق فيه
When He intended waiting He did not say 'to'. When He, glorified, said: 'to their Lord looking' we knew that He did not intend waiting, but rather intended the gaze of vision. And when Allah coupled 'looking' with the mention of 'face', He intended the look of the two eyes that are in the face, as He said: 'We have seen the turning of your face in the heaven, so We will surely turn you to a qibla that pleases you' [Al-Baqarah 2:144]; He mentioned the face, but He only intended the turning of his eyes towards the heaven waiting for the descent of the angel to him concerning Allah's turning him from the qibla of Jerusalem to the Kaaba. If someone says: Why do you not say that His statement 'to their Lord looking' meant 'looking towards the reward of her Lord'? It is said to him: The reward of Allah, mighty and majestic, is other than Him, exalted, and Allah, glorified and exalted, said: 'to their Lord looking' and did not say 'to other than Him looking'. And the noble Quran is upon its apparent meaning, and it is not for us to remove it from its apparent meaning except with a proof; otherwise it is upon its apparent meaning. Do you not see that when Allah, mighty and majestic, said: 'Pray to Me and worship Me', it is not permissible for anyone to say: He intended other than Him and remove the speech from its apparent meaning? Therefore, when He said: 'to their Lord looking', it is not permissible for us to remove the Quran from its apparent meaning without proof. Then it is said to the Mu'tazila: If it is permissible for you to claim that the statement of Allah, mighty and majestic, 'and to their Lord looking' meant that they are looking at other than Him, then why is it not permissible for someone else to say that the statement of Allah, mighty and majestic, 'sight does not perceive Him' [Al-An'am 6:103] meant that it does not perceive other than Him, and He did not intend that it does not perceive Him? And this is something they are unable to differentiate.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument from al-Ash'ari defending the Sunni position that believers will see Allah in the afterlife. First, he analyzes the Quranic phrase 'إلى ربها ناظرة' (to their Lord looking). He argues that the word 'ناظرة' here means 'seeing' not 'waiting', because if it meant waiting, the preposition 'إلى' would not have been used; rather, the verb 'انتظر' (to wait) is followed by 'إلى' only when the object of waiting is mentioned? Actually he says 'فلما أراد الانتظار لم يقل إلى' (when He intended waiting He did not say 'to')—this is a grammatical point that the word 'ناظرة' when used for waiting is not followed by 'إلى' in this way. The verse uses 'إلى' indicating direction, and coupled with the mention of 'الوجه' (face) in other verses, al-Ash'ari argues it refers to the physical eyes seeing. He cites the verse about the Prophet turning his face to heaven, noting that 'وجه' is mentioned but the intended meaning is the turning of his eyes. He then addresses an objection: could the verse mean 'looking at the reward of their Lord'? Al-Ash'ari responds that the reward is other than Allah, and the verse says 'to their Lord', not 'to other than Him'. He establishes the principle that the Quran is to be taken on its apparent meaning unless there is a proof to remove it. He gives an example: if Allah had said 'Pray to Me and worship Me', no one could reinterpret it as meaning other than Him. So too with this verse. He then turns the tables on the Mu'tazila: if they can reinterpret 'to their Lord looking' to mean something else, then others could reinterpret 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (sight does not perceive Him) to mean 'it does not perceive other than Him', and they would have no way to distinguish. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum, showing the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila position.

Key Terms
نظر (looking/seeing)
ظاهر (apparent meaning)
رؤية (vision)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'ناظرة' in the verse means 'waiting' rather than 'seeing'.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that the use of the preposition 'إلى' and the context of 'face' indicate that 'looking' here means visual seeing, not waiting.

Checkpoint
What grammatical point does al-Ash'ari make about the word 'ناظرة' in the verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة'?
A) The word 'ناظرة' is followed by the preposition 'إلى', which indicates direction and vision, not waiting.
B) The word 'ناظرة' is always used for waiting in the Quran.
C) The word 'ناظرة' cannot be used for seeing because it is feminine.
D) The word 'ناظرة' must be interpreted metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari argues that the Quran must be interpreted according to its apparent meaning, and that the verse about seeing Allah is no exception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a clear command or description in the Quran, do you instinctively accept it as it is, or do you find yourself explaining it away to suit your comfort? What is one verse you have reinterpreted to avoid its full weight?
?
Al-Ash'ari insists that the Quran is on its apparent meaning unless there is proof. Is there an area of your life where you have changed the meaning of Allah's words without solid evidence? What would it cost you to submit to the apparent meaning?
?
If the Mu'tazila could be inconsistent in their reinterpretation, could you be doing the same? Think of a belief you hold that you think is 'rational' but that contradicts a plain verse. Are you willing to let Allah's speech override your reasoning, even if it feels uncomfortable?

The Proof from Moses' Request to See Allah
Al-Ash'ari uses Moses' request to prove vision is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari uses the example of Prophet Moses to argue for the possibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
Moses would not ask Allah for the impossible, so vision must be possible.

Al-Ash'ari presents a second proof for the beatific vision, this time drawing on the Qur'anic story of Prophet Moses. Moses' request to see Allah (Q. 7:143) is not a mistake; as a protected prophet, his request shows that seeing Allah is not impossible. The argument also deflects the Mu'tazilite claim that vision is impossible, by showing their position would imply they know more than Moses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the verse reference and how Al-Ash'ari immediately interprets it.
2
Identify the key premise
Underline the premise that prophets are infallible (عصمة). Al-Ash'ari states that Moses would not ask for something impossible because Allah protects prophets.
This is a cornerstone of Sunni theology.
3
Understand the logical chain
Trace the logic: Moses asked (fact) → prophets do not ask for impossibles (premise) → therefore vision is possible (conclusion).
4
Consider the counter-argument
Read how Al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazila: if vision were impossible and Moses didn't know it, then the Mu'tazila would be claiming superior knowledge over a prophet, which is absurd.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does Al-Ash'ari use a prophet's request as evidence? This highlights the respect for prophetic authority in Sunni creed.
The proof relies on the integrity of prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧٤ ودليل آخر : ومما يدل على أن الله تعالى يرى بالأبصار قول موسى : رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣] . ولا يجوز أن يكون موسى صلوات الله عليه وسلامه الذي قد ألبسه الله تعالى جلباب النبيين، وعصمه بما عصم به المرسلين قد سأل ربه ما يستحيل عليه، وإذا لم يجز ذلك على موسى فقد علمنا أنه لم يسأل ربه مستحيلاً، وأن الرؤية جائزة على ربنا عزَّ وجلَّ . ولو كانت الرؤية مستحيلة على ربنا تعالى كما زعمت المعتزلة، ولم يعلم ذلك عليه الصلاة والسلام وعلموا هم ، لكانوا على قولهم موسى أعلم بالله من موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام، وهذا مما لا يدعيه مسلم .
74. Another proof: Among what indicates that Allah, exalted, is seen with the eyes is the saying of Moses: 'My Lord, show me [that I may] look at You' [Al-A'raf 7:143]. It is not permissible that Moses, peace and blessings of Allah be upon him, whom Allah has clothed with the garment of the prophets and protected as He protected the messengers, would have asked his Lord for something impossible. And if that is not permissible of Moses, then we know that he did not ask his Lord for an impossibility, and that vision is possible of our Lord, mighty and majestic. And if vision were impossible for our Lord, exalted, as the Mu'tazila claim, and he (peace be upon him) did not know that while they know it, then according to their claim Moses would be less knowledgeable of Allah than Moses (peace be upon him), and this is something no Muslim would claim.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents a second proof for the visibility of Allah. Al-Ash'ari cites the verse in which Moses asks Allah, 'رب أرني أنظر إليك' (My Lord, show me that I may look at You). He argues from the status of Moses as a prophet and messenger, protected by Allah from error and sin. It is inconceivable that Moses would ask for something that is impossible for Allah (i.e., to see Him), because a prophet does not ask for what is impossible. Therefore, since Moses asked, the vision of Allah must be possible. He then turns the argument against the Mu'tazila: if they claim vision is impossible, and that Moses was ignorant of this while they know it, then they are claiming to be more knowledge about Allah than Moses—which is an unacceptable claim for any Muslim. The logic is straightforward: either Moses knew the ruling or he did not. If he knew, then it is possible; if he did not, then the Mu'tazila are claiming superiority over a prophet, which is false. Hence, the vision is possible and will occur.

Key Terms
رؤية (vision)
استحالة (impossibility)
موسى (Moses)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Moses' request was a mistake or a sin, implying he asked for something impossible.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari emphasizes that prophets are protected (عصمة) from such errors; therefore Moses' request proves possibility, not ignorance.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ash'ari use Moses' request as proof that vision of Allah is possible?
A) Because Moses is a prophet and would not ask for something impossible.
B) Because the verse explicitly says that Moses saw Allah.
C) Because the Mu'tazila agree that Moses asked for vision.
D) Because Moses was the greatest prophet.

Key Takeaway
Moses' request to see Allah demonstrates that seeing Allah is possible, because a protected prophet would not ask for the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Moses, a prophet who spoke directly to Allah, humbly pleaded to see Him, does your heart yearn for that vision with the same intensity? What distracts you from nurturing that longing?
?
Al-Ash'ari says no Muslim would claim to be more knowledgeable about Allah than Moses. When you rely on your own reasoning over clear revelation, are you in danger of that same arrogance?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they knew better than a prophet. Is there any belief you hold that you feel is 'obvious' even though it contradicts what Allah has promised? Will you submit to Allah's words or your own intellect?

Proof from Musa's Request for Ru'ya
Imam Ashari argues from Musa's request for ru'ya to prove its possibility and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ashari uses Prophet Musa's request to see Allah as evidence that seeing Allah (ru'ya) is possible, and why this refutes the Mu'tazila claim of impossibility.

Core Idea
Musa's request proves ru'ya is possible because prophets do not ask for the impossible.

Imam Ashari draws on a key Qur'anic moment—Musa's request to see Allah—to demonstrate that vision of Allah is rationally possible. Since prophets are protected from error, they would not ask for something impossible. The passage then addresses a potential objection to strengthen the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the argument structure: verse, premise, conclusion, then an objection and answer.
The verse is from Surah al-A'raf, verse 143.
2
Identify the core argument
Imam Ashari argues that Musa's request to see Allah must have been for something possible, because prophets are divinely protected from asking for impossibilities. Therefore, ru'ya is possible.
This is a straightforward proof from prophetic infallibility (ismah).
3
Understand the reductio against the Mu'tazila
If ru'ya were impossible (as Mu'tazila claim) and Musa did not know that, then the Mu'tazila would be claiming greater knowledge than Musa—an absurdity.
This is a 'reductio ad absurdum' argument.
4
Examine the zihar objection and Ashari's answer
An objector asks: why did the Prophet not know the ruling of zihar before revelation? Ashari replies that rulings become obligatory only at their time, whereas ru'ya's possibility is eternal. The Mu'tazila's claim still implies they know more than Musa.
This distinguishes temporal knowledge from inherent attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣] . ولا يجوز أن يكون موسى صلوات الله عليه وسلامه الذي قد ألبسه الله تعالى جلباب النبيين، وعصمه بما عصم به المرسلين قد سأل ربه ما يستحيل عليه، وإذا لم يجز ذلك على موسى فقد علمنا أنه لم يسأل ربه مستحيلاً، وأن الرؤية جائزة على ربنا عزَّ وجلَّ .
ولو كانت الرؤية مستحيلة على ربنا تعالى كما زعمت المعتزلة، ولم يعلم ذلك عليه الصلاة والسلام وعلموا هم ، لكانوا على قولهم موسى أعلم بالله من موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام، وهذا مما لا يدعيه مسلم .
-٧٥ فإن قال قائل : ألستم تعلمون حكم الله في الظهار اليوم، ولم يكن نبي الله يعلم ذلك قبل أن ينزل؟
قيل له : لم يكن يعلم نبي الله ذلك قبل أن يلزم الله العباد حكم الظهار، فلما لزمهم الحكم به أعلم نبيه قبلهم ، ثم أعلم نبي الله عباد الله ذلك، ولم يأت عليه وقته لزمه حكمه، فلم يعلمه عليه الصلاة والسلام . وأنتم زعمتم أن موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام كان قد لزمه أن يعلم حكم الرؤية، وأنها مستحيلة عليه، وإذا لم يعلم ذلك وقت أن لزمه علمه، وعلمتموه أنتم الآن، لزمكم بجهلكم ، أنكم بما لزمكم العلم به الآن أعلم من موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام بما لزمه العلم ،به وهذا خروج عن دين المسلمين .
“My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You” [al-A‘raf 7:143]. And it is not permissible that Musa—peace and blessings of Allah be upon him—whom Allah Almighty has clothed with the robe of the prophets and protected with what He protected the messengers, should have asked his Lord for something impossible for Him. And if that is not permissible of Musa, then we know that he did not ask his Lord for something impossible, and that vision (al-ru’ya) is possible (ja’iz) for our Lord, Mighty and Majestic. And if vision were impossible (mustahil) for our Lord Almighty as the Mu‘tazila claim, and he—peace be upon him—did not know that, while they know it, then according to their statement they would be more knowledgeable of Allah than Musa—peace be upon him—and this is something no Muslim claims. -75 If someone says: Do you not know the ruling of Allah concerning zihar today, while the Prophet of Allah did not know that before it was revealed? It is said to him: The Prophet of Allah did not know that before Allah obligated the servants with the ruling of zihar; when He obligated them with the ruling, He informed His Prophet before them, then the Prophet of Allah informed the servants of Allah of that. And it had not come upon its time for him to be obligated by its ruling, so he—peace be upon him—did not know it. But you claim that Musa—peace be upon him—was obligated to know the ruling of vision, and that it is impossible for Him. And if he did not know that at the time he was obligated to know it, and you know it now, then it follows from your ignorance that you, by what you are obligated to know now, are more knowledgeable than Musa—peace be upon him—concerning what he was obligated to know. And this is a departure from the religion of the Muslims.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru’ya) in the afterlife, followed by an objection-and-answer. The proof is based on the request of Prophet Musa (Moses) in Quran 7:143. Imam Ashari argues that it is inconceivable for a prophet—protected by divine ‘isma—to ask his Lord for something impossible. Since Musa did ask, the request must be for something possible; therefore, ru’ya is possible for Allah. The author then refutes the Mu‘tazila claim that ru’ya is impossible by a reductio ad absurdum: if ru’ya were impossible and Musa did not know that, but the Mu‘tazila claim to know it now, then they would be claiming greater knowledge of Allah than Musa—an absurdity no Muslim would accept. Next, an objector raises an analogy: today we know the ruling of zihar (a form of divorce) but the Prophet did not know it before revelation. The answer clarifies the difference: rulings can become known after their time, whereas ru’ya is about inherent possibility. The Prophet’s knowledge of ru’ya’s possibility was not required until the time it became relevant, but the Mu‘tazila’s claim that they now know an impossibility that Musa did not know would still imply their superiority, which is rejected.

Key Terms
رؤية
جائز
مستحيل
المعتزلة
الظهار
عصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a prophet did not know something at a particular time, it implies that ordinary people can know more than the prophet about that matter.
Correction: Imam Ashari explains that knowledge of contingent rulings depends on the timing of obligation, whereas knowledge of divine attributes like the possibility of ru'ya is not time-bound. The Mu'tazila's claim of impossibility would still entail claiming superiority over Musa, which is invalid.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Ashari argue that Musa's request in Quran 7:143 proves ru'ya is possible?
A) Because Musa was a prophet and prophets never ask for impossible things.
B) Because the verse explicitly says ru'ya is wajib.
C) Because the Mu'tazila agree it is possible.
D) Because the mountain actually moved.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Musa's request to see Allah, combined with his infallibility, provides a decisive proof that vision of Allah is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you will see Allah in Paradise? If so, how does that hope shape your actions today? If not, what is holding you back from accepting the clear proof from Musa's request? Commit to reciting the verse 'Rabbi arini anthur ilayka' daily until your certainty is firm.
?
The Mu'tazila put their reasoning above the apparent meaning of the Qur'an and the example of the prophets. Where in your own life are you tempted to prioritize your intellect over divine revelation? Identify one area and repent from that arrogance today.
?
Prophet Musa asked to see Allah because he knew it was possible. When you ask Allah for something in your du'a, do you limit yourself to what is possible for Him? Or do you sometimes doubt His power? Strengthen your du'a by asking for the greatest blessing: to see His Face in the hereafter.

Second Proof from the Conditional Verse
Imam Ashari presents the proof from the conditional verse and refutes the deferral interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari uses the conditional structure of Quran 7:143 to prove the possibility of seeing Allah, and how he refutes the interpretation that the condition implies denial.

Core Idea
Allah linked vision to a possible condition, not an impossible one, proving it is possible.

Imam al-Ash'ari presents a second Quranic proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, rooted in the conditional verse addressed to Musa. He anticipates an objection that the condition might be a deferral (tab'id) and refutes it using Arabic linguistic conventions, including a famous example from the poet al-Khansa'. This lesson examines his argument and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof from the verse
Notice how Ash'ari draws the inference: Allah can make the mountain stay, so He can cause Musa to see Him.
2
Examine the objection
The objector claims the condition implies deferral, meaning you will never see Me.
3
Analyze the refutation
Ash'ari argues that deferral would be expressed by an impossible condition, but settling of the mountain is possible.
4
Consider the example of al-Khansa'
She used an impossible condition to express refusal. This shows Arabic usage.
This is a key linguistic proof.
5
Conclude the lesson
Ash'ari concludes that ru'ya is ja'iz because the condition is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل آخر :
مما يدل على جواز رؤية الله تعالى بالأبصار قول الله تعالى لموسى صلى الله عليه وسلم : ( فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوفَ تركني ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣] . فلما كان الله عز وجل قادراً على أن يجعل الجبل مستقراً، كان قادراً على الأمر الذي لو فعله لرآه موسى صلى الله عليه وسلم، فدل ذلـك علـى أن الله تعالى قادر أن يُري عباده نفسه، وأنه جائز رؤيته .
فإن قال قائل : فلم لا قلتم إن قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ، فَسَوْفَ تركني ) [ الأعراف : ١٤٣] تبعيد للرؤية؟ قيل له : لو أراد الله عز وجل تبعيد الرؤية لقرن الكلام بما يستحيل وقوعه، ولم يقرنه بما يجوز وقوعه، فلما قرنه باستقرار الجبل، وذلك أمر مقدور الله سبحانه وتعالى، دل ذلك على أنه جائز أن يُرى الله عز وجل .
ألا ترى أن الخنساء لما أرادت تبعيد صلحها لمن كان حرباً لأخيها قرنت الكلام بأمر مستحيل، فقالت: ولا أُصالِحُ قَومَا كُنتُ حَرْبهُمُ حتَّى تَعُودَ بياضاً حُلْكَة القَارِ والله عز وجل إنما خاطب العرب بلغتها، تجده مفهوماً في كلامها، ومعقولاً في خطابها
فلما قرن الله الرؤية بأمر مقدور جائز، علمنا أن رؤية الله بالأبصار جائزة غير مستحيلة .
And another proof: Among what indicates the possibility of seeing Allah Almighty with the eyes is the statement of Allah Almighty to Musa, peace be upon him: “But if it remains in its place, then you will see Me” [al-A‘raf 7:143]. Since Allah Almighty is able to make the mountain remain in place, He is able to do that which, if He did it, Musa, peace be upon him, would see Him. So that indicates that Allah Almighty is able to show Himself to His servants, and that seeing Him is possible (ja’iz). If someone says: Why do you not say that the statement of Allah Almighty “But if it remains in its place, then you will see Me” is a deferral (tab‘id) of vision? It is said to him: If Allah Almighty intended deferral of vision, He would have linked the speech to something impossible to occur, and He would not have linked it to something possible to occur. Since He linked it to the settling of the mountain, and that is a matter within the power (maqdur) of Allah, Subhanahu wa Ta‘ala, that indicates that it is possible for Allah Almighty to be seen. Do you not see that al-Khansa’, when she intended to defer reconciliation with those who were at war with her brother, linked the speech to an impossible matter, saying: “I will not make peace with a people I was at war with until black tar becomes white.” And Allah Almighty addressed the Arabs in their language; you find this understood in their speech and reasonable in their discourse. So when Allah linked vision to a possible, powerful matter, we know that seeing Allah with the eyes is possible (ja’iza), not impossible (mustahila).
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is another proof (dalil) for the possibility of seeing Allah, followed by an objection-and-answer. The proof is from the same Quranic verse (7:143) but focuses on the conditional structure: “if the mountain remains in its place, then you will see Me.” Ashari argues that Allah is able to make the mountain remain, so He is able to bring about the condition; thus He is able to cause Musa to see Him. Therefore, ru’ya is possible. An objector then claims that the verse could be interpreted as a deferral (tab‘id) meaning “you will not see Me because the mountain will not remain.” The answer distinguishes between Arabic usage: if Allah intended to deny or defer, He would have linked the result to something impossible, not to something possible (like the mountain settling). He gives the example of the poet al-Khansa’, who, to express her firm refusal to reconcile, swore by an impossible condition (black tar turning white). Allah, speaking in Arabic, used a possible condition, indicating that vision is indeed possible when the condition is met. Thus, the objection fails, and ru’ya remains ja’iz.

Key Terms
رؤية
جواز
تبعيد
مستحيل
مقدور
الخنساء
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret the conditional in 7:143 as a denial of ru'ya; they think that because the condition is unlikely, it implies impossibility.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that Arabic speakers use impossible conditions for denial, not possible ones. Since the mountain settling is possible (within Allah's power), the condition is not a denial.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Ashari use the poetry of al-Khansa' to refute the claim that the verse is a deferral of ru'ya?
A) She used an impossible condition to express deferral, while Allah used a possible condition.
B) She was a poetess and her poetry is not relevant to Quranic interpretation.
C) She argued that ru'ya is possible.
D) She used a possible condition to express deferral.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing Allah is affirmed because the condition in the verse is something within Allah's power, not an impossibility, and this is consistent with Arabic usage where denial is expressed by impossible conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'if the mountain remains, you will see Me,' do you honestly expect to see Allah in the afterlife, or have you allowed doubts to creep in that make you treat it as an impossible hope? How would your daily actions change if you were certain that you will see Him?
?
The poetess al-Khansa' used an impossible condition to express her refusal to reconcile. Are there areas in your life where you use impossible conditions—like 'I'll pray when I have time' or 'I'll repent when I'm older'—as a way to defer obedience? What is one concrete step you can take today to remove that condition?
?
If seeing Allah is truly possible, what does that mean for your longing for Him? Do you yearn for that vision more than anything else, or have you become satisfied with lesser pleasures? What can you do this week to increase your desire to meet your Lord?

Quranic Proof for Seeing Allah
Proof from Musa's request and refutation of deterrent objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse about Musa's request serves as a proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah, and how the objection of deterrence is refuted using Arabic linguistic custom.

Core Idea
Allah's conditional promise to Musa proves seeing Him is possible.

This lesson presents a key Quranic proof used by Ahl al-Sunnah for the permissibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. The argument turns on the nature of the condition Allah set for Musa. The passage also refutes the objection that the verse was merely a deterrent, using an example from classical Arabic poetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and inference
Read the verse from Surah Al-A'raf and note how the author infers that if Allah can settle the mountain, He can enable vision.
The verse is conditional: 'If it settles, you will see Me.'
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims the verse is a deterrent, meaning 'you will never see Me.' Consider why someone might think this.
Deterrence uses impossible conditions.
3
Follow the refutation
The refutation explains that Allah linked vision to a possible event (mountain settling), not an impossible one, so it indicates possibility.
The example from Al-Khansa' illustrates deterrent speech.
4
Reflect on the example
Read Al-Khansa' verse: 'I will not reconcile until black pitch becomes white.' Notice the impossible condition.
Allah addressed Arabs in their own linguistic custom.
5
Conclude the doctrine
Affirm that seeing Allah with the eyes is permissible and not impossible, based on this proof.
This is a standard Ash'ari proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل آخر : مما يدل على جواز رؤية الله تعالى بالأبصار قول الله تعالى لموسى صلى الله عليه وسلم : ﴿ فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوفَ تركني ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣] . فلما كان الله عز وجل قادراً على أن يجعل الجبل مستقراً، كان قادراً على الأمر الذي لو فعله لرآه موسى صلى الله عليه وسلم، فدل ذلـك علـى أن الله تعالى قادر أن يُري عباده نفسه، وأنه جائز رؤيته . فإن قال قائل : فلم لا قلتم إن قول الله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ، فَسَوْفَ تركني ) [ الأعراف : ١٤٣] تبعيد للرؤية؟ قيل له : لو أراد الله عز وجل تبعيد الرؤية لقرن الكلام بما يستحيل وقوعه، ولم يقرنه بما يجوز وقوعه، فلما قرنه باستقرار الجبل، وذلك أمر مقدور الله سبحانه وتعالى، دل ذلك على أنه جائز أن يُرى الله عز وجل . ألا ترى أن الخنساء لما أرادت تبعيد صلحها لمن كان حرباً لأخيها قرنت الكلام بأمر مستحيل، فقالت(۱): ولا أُصالِحُ قَومَا كُنتُ حَرْبهُمُ حتَّى تَعُودَ بياضاً حُلْكَة القَارِ والله عز وجل إنما خاطب العرب بلغتها، تجده مفهوماً في كلامها، ومعقولاً في خطابها فلما قرن الله الرؤية بأمر مقدور جائز، علمنا أن رؤية الله بالأبصار جائزة غير مستحيلة
Another proof: Among what indicates the permissibility of seeing Allah Most High with the eyes is the saying of Allah Most High to Musa, peace be upon him: 'So if it settles in its place, you will see Me' [Al-A'raf 7:143]. Since Allah Almighty is able to make the mountain settle, He is able to do that which if He did, Musa would see Him, so that indicates that Allah is able to show Himself to His servants, and that seeing Him is permissible. If someone says: Why do you not say that Allah's saying 'So if it settles in its place, you will see Me' is a deterrence from seeing? It is said to him: If Allah intended deterrence from seeing, He would have linked the speech to something impossible to occur, and He did not link it to something possible to occur. Since He linked it to the settling of the mountain, and that is a matter within Allah's power, that indicates that it is permissible that Allah be seen. Do you not see that Al-Khansa', when she intended to deter reconciliation with those who were at war with her brother, linked the speech to an impossible thing, saying: 'I will not reconcile with a people I was at war with until black pitch becomes white.' And Allah Almighty only addressed the Arabs in their language; you find this understood in their speech and comprehended in their discourse. So when Allah linked vision to a possible, feasible matter, we know that seeing Allah with the eyes is permissible, not impossible.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya) with the eyes, followed by an objection and response. The author first cites the verse where Allah tells Musa: 'If the mountain settles in its place, you will see Me.' Since settling the mountain is within Allah's power, He is able to show Himself to Musa, proving that seeing Allah is possible. The objection then raises that the verse might be a deterrent (tab'id) — i.e., meaning 'you will never see Me' — as a way of discouraging the request. The response refutes this by arguing that deterrent speech in Arabic customarily links the condition to something impossible (muhal), not to something possible (ja'iz). Since settling the mountain is possible for Allah, the condition indicates possibility. The author gives an example from the poet Al-Khansa', who used an impossible condition ('until black pitch becomes white') to express her firm refusal. This shows that if Allah had intended deterrence, He would have used an impossible condition. Thus, the verse actually proves that vision is possible. The conclusion: seeing Allah with the eyes is permissible, not impossible.

Key Terms
رؤية
تبعيد
جواز
استحالة
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'If the mountain settles, you will see Me' is an absolute denial, meaning Musa will never see Allah.
Correction: The conditional nature of the verse actually indicates possibility, because the condition is something Allah can do. If it were a denial, the condition would be impossible, as shown by Arabic linguistic custom.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that the verse about Musa's request indicates the permissibility of seeing Allah?
A) Because the mountain actually settled.
B) Because Allah linked the vision to a possible act, not an impossible one.
C) Because Musa was a prophet and prophets can see Allah.
D) Because Al-Khansa' used a similar condition.

Key Takeaway
Allah's conditional promise to Musa shows that seeing Him is possible because the condition is within His power, and Arabic custom confirms that possible conditions indicate possibility, not deterrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you long to see Allah with the same intensity as Musa? Or has your heart become numb to this ultimate goal? What would it take for you to truly yearn for the vision of your Lord?
?
When you face doubts about theological matters, do you approach them with the linguistic precision of the scholars, or do you let ambiguities shake your faith? How can you train yourself to think more carefully about divine attributes?
?
The verse shows that Allah is willing to show Himself to His believing servants. If you truly believed that you will see Allah in the afterlife, how would it change the way you worship and live today? Are you preparing for that moment?

Proof for the Vision of Allah and Reconciliation of Apparent Contradiction
Cites Quranic verses supporting vision and explains the verse denying comprehension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ash'ari proves the vision of Allah in the afterlife using multiple Quranic verses and reconciles the apparent contrary verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'.

Core Idea
Quranic verses affirm believers see Allah; denial refers to this world or disbelievers.

Imam Ash'ari presents another proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah, citing several verses that exegetes interpret as referring to the vision of Allah in Paradise. He then anticipates an objection from the famous verse 'Vision comprehends Him not' and offers two plausible interpretations that harmonize with the affirming verses. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof verses
Read the four Quranic verses cited by Imam Ash'ari and their interpretations as referring to the vision of Allah. Notice how each verse is connected to the theme of seeing Allah.
The verses include 10:26, 50:35, 33:44, and 83:15.
2
Understand the objection
The objection cites the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (6:103), which apparently denies any vision of Allah. Recognize this as a potential counter-argument.
3
Explore the two reconciliations
Imam Ash'ari gives two possibilities: either the denial applies only to this world, or it applies only to the eyes of disbelievers. Reflect on how each harmonizes with the affirming verses.
The second interpretation relies on the principle that the Quran interprets itself.
4
Appreciate the principle of Quranic coherence
Notice how Imam Ash'ari argues that the Quran confirms some parts with others, so verses promising vision and the verse denying comprehension must be reconciled, not taken in isolation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل آخر : قال الله عز وجل : لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الْحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ [ يونس : ٢٦]. قال أهل التأويل : النظر إلى الله عز وجل، ولم يُنْعِم الله عز وجل على أهل جنانه بأفضل من نظرهم إليه ورؤيتهم له. وقال عز وجل: (وَلَدَيْنَا مَزِيدٌ ﴾ [ق:٣٥]. قيل: النظر إلى الله عز وجل. وقال : ( تَحِيَّتُهُم يوم يلقونَهُ سَلَمٌ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٤٤] وإذا لقيه المؤمنون رأوه. وقال الله عز وجل : كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَذٍ مَحْجُوبُونَ ﴾ [المطففين: ١٥] فحجبهم عن رؤيته، ولا يحجب عنها المؤمنين . سؤال : فإن قال قائل : فما معنى قوله تعالى: (لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَرُ ) ؟ [الأنعام: ١٠٣] . قيل له : يحتمل أن يكون : لا تدركه في الدنيا وتدركه في الآخرة ؛ لأن رؤية الله تعالى أفضل اللذات وأفضل اللذات تكون في أفضل الدارين . ويحتمل أن يكون الله عز وجل أراد بقوله : ( لا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ) يعني : لا تدركه أبصار الكافرين المكذبين، وذلك أن كتاب الله يُصَدِّقُ بعضه بعضاً، فلما قال في آية : إن الوجوه تنظر إليه يوم القيامة، وقال في آية أخرى : إن الأبصار لا تدركه علمنا أنه إنما أراد أبصار الكفار لا تدركه
Another proof: Allah Almighty said: 'For those who do good is the best [reward] and more' [Yunus 10:26]. The people of interpretation said: 'The best is looking at Allah Almighty, and Allah Almighty has not bestowed upon the people of His Paradise anything better than their looking at Him and seeing Him.' And He Almighty said: 'And with Us is more' [Qaf 50:35]. It was said: 'Looking at Allah Almighty.' And He said: 'Their greeting on the day they meet Him is peace' [Al-Ahzab 33:44]. And when the believers meet Him, they will see Him. And Allah Almighty said: 'No! Indeed, from their Lord that Day they are veiled' [Al-Mutaffifin 83:15]. So He veiled them from seeing Him, and He does not veil the believers from it. A question: If someone says: What is the meaning of His statement: 'Vision comprehends Him not' [Al-An'am 6:103]? It is said to him: It is possible that it means: They do not comprehend Him in this world but will comprehend Him in the Hereafter, because seeing Allah is the greatest pleasure, and the greatest pleasure occurs in the best of the two abodes. And it is possible that Allah Almighty intended by His statement 'Vision comprehends Him not' meaning: the eyes of the disbelieving deniers do not comprehend Him, because the Book of Allah confirms some parts of it others. So since He said in one verse that faces will look at Him on the Day of Resurrection, and He said in another verse that vision does not comprehend Him, we know that He only meant the eyes of the disbelievers do not comprehend Him.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents another proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah, citing multiple Quranic verses, followed by an objection and answer. The author first cites Surah Yunus 10:26, where 'al-husna' (the best reward) is interpreted by exegetes as Paradise, and 'ziyadah' (more) as the vision of Allah (al-nazar ila Allah). This vision is described as the greatest blessing for the people of Paradise. Then Surah Qaf 50:35, where 'mazid' (more) is also interpreted as looking at Allah. Then Surah Al-Ahzab 33:44, where 'meeting Him' implies vision. Then Surah Al-Mutaffifin 83:15, where the disbelievers are veiled from their Lord, implying that believers are not veiled, so they see Him. The objection then raises the apparent counter-verse from Surah Al-An'am 6:103: 'Vision comprehends Him not' (la tudrikuhu al-absar). The author gives two possible interpretations: First, that this refers to the present world (dunya) only, while in the Hereafter believers will see Him. This is supported by the principle that the greatest pleasure belongs in the best abode (the Hereafter). Second, that 'the eyes' here specifies the eyes of the disbelievers, because the Quran interprets itself: verses promising vision are about believers, while the denial of comprehension applies to disbelievers. The author concludes that the verse does not negate vision for believers in the Hereafter.

Key Terms
الحسنى
زيادة
مزيد
النظر إلى الله
لا تدركه الأبصار
الحجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' absolutely denies any possibility of seeing Allah, even in the afterlife.
Correction: Imam Ash'ari shows that the verse is either restricted to this world or to the eyes of disbelievers, and does not negate the vision for believers in Paradise.

Checkpoint
How does the author reconcile the verse 'Vision comprehends Him not' with verses promising vision?
A) By claiming the verse is abrogated.
B) By interpreting it as referring to this world or to the eyes of disbelievers.
C) By saying it means only the mind cannot comprehend Him.
D) By denying that any verse promises vision.

Key Takeaway
Imam Ash'ari affirms the vision of Allah for believers in the afterlife by interpreting the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' as specific to this world or to the disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeing Allah is the greatest pleasure of Paradise, how does your daily pursuit of pleasures reflect your longing for that ultimate vision? Are you investing in things that will bring you closer to that moment or distract you from it?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to contradict another, do you immediately dismiss one or do you seek reconciliation like Imam Ash'ari did? What does your approach reveal about your trust in the coherence of the Quran?
?
The disbelievers are described as 'veiled' from their Lord. Consider your own heart: are there any veils of heedlessness or sin that prevent you from experiencing the reality of Allah now? What concrete step can you take today to remove one such veil?

The True Reason for Condemning the Request for Vision
Refutes the objection that condemned request proves vision impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's condemnation of the Children of Israel's request to see Him does not prove that seeing Allah is impossible.

Core Idea
Condemnation of a request does not imply the requested thing is impossible.

Some argue that because Allah rebuked the Children of Israel for asking to see Him, seeing Allah must be impossible. But this passage clarifies that the condemnation was directed at their insolence and disbelief, not at the act of vision itself. Let's examine the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the Quranic quotes embedded in the text.
2
Identify the objection being refuted
The objection is that since Allah condemned the request for vision, vision must be impossible. Find where this objection is implied in the passage.
The passage begins by stating the objection: 'Moses was greater than that, so they said...'
3
Note the parallel with the request for a book
The author draws an analogy: just as Allah condemned the People of the Book for demanding a book from heaven but that does not make sending a book impossible, so too with vision.
This parallel is key to understanding the refutation.
4
Understand the key distinction
The condemnation is about the manner and motive (disbelief), not the thing requested. Recognize that the request for vision was made 'by way of denying the prophethood of Moses'.
The thing requested (vision) is not inherently wrong or impossible.
5
Reflect on the implication for the possibility of seeing Allah
Conclude that this passage supports the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife, as the condemnation does not negate the act itself.
This is a foundational argument in Islamic theology about the beatific vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موسَى أَكْبَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَقَالُوا اَرِنَا اللَّهَ جَهْرَةً ) [النساء : ١٥٣] .
فيقال لهم : إن بني إسرائيل سألوا رؤية الله عز وجل على طريق الإنكار لنبوة موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام، وترك الإيمان به حتى يروا الله ؛ لأنهم قالوا : لَن تُؤْمِنَ لَكَ حَتَّى نَرَى الله جَهْرَةً ) [البقرة : 00] .
فلما سألوه الرؤية عن طريق ترك الإيمان بموسى عليه السلام حتى يرىهم الله نفسه؛ استعظم الله سؤالهم من غير أن تكون الرؤية مستحيلة عليه، كما استعظم سؤال أهل الكتاب أن ينزل عليهم كتاباً من السماء من غير أن يكون ذلك مستحيلاً، ولكن لأنهم أبوا أن يؤمنوا بنبي الله [صلى الله عليه وسلم] حتى ينزل عليهم كتاباً من السماء .
Moses was greater than that, so they said, 'Show us Allah outright' [an-Nisa 4:153]. So it is said to them: The Children of Israel asked to see Allah Almighty by way of denying the prophethood of Moses, peace and blessings be upon him, and refraining from believing in him until they see Allah, because they said, 'We will not believe you until we see Allah outright' [al-Baqarah 2:55]. When they asked for the vision by way of refraining from belief in Moses until Allah Himself showed them, Allah deemed their question great not because the vision is impossible for Him, just as He deemed great the People of the Book's request that a book be sent down to them from heaven not because that is impossible, but because they refused to believe in the prophet of Allah until a book is sent down to them from heaven.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection raised by those who deny the possibility of seeing Allah. The objectors cite the story of the Children of Israel who demanded to see Allah and were punished or rebuked, arguing that this proves vision is impossible. The author explains that Allah's condemnation of their request was due to their insolence and disbelief in Moses, not because vision is inherently impossible. He draws a parallel: Allah also condemned the People of the Book for demanding a book from heaven, yet no one claims that sending a book is impossible. Thus, the condemnation is about the manner and motive, not the act itself.

إنكار
نبوة
استعظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's condemnation of a request for vision implies vision is impossible.
Correction: The condemnation was due to the disbelief and insolence of the requesters, not because the thing requested (vision) is impossible in itself.

Checkpoint
Why did Allah deem the request of the Children of Israel great?
A) Because seeing Allah is impossible in itself.
B) Because they demanded vision while disbelieving in Moses.
C) Because they were not prophets.
D) Because they asked for a miracle.

Key Takeaway
The condemnation of a request does not entail the impossibility of the requested thing; rather, it may be due to the insolence and disbelief of the requester.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for something, do you check your own heart for arrogance or disbelief behind the request? How often do you condition your faith on seeing results first?
?
The Children of Israel demanded to see Allah to believe. What are the 'sights' you are demanding from Allah before you fully submit—money, success, clear signs? Will you wait until you see, or will you believe now?
?
Allah condemned their request because of their refusal to believe in His prophet. Are there any ways you dismiss or doubt the messengers of Allah today—through indifference, criticism, or selective obedience? What would it take for you to accept their message wholeheartedly?

Hadith Proof for Vision of Allah on the Moon Analogy
Al-Ash'ari cites the hadith of seeing Allah like the moon and argues for its reliability via multiple chains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Ash'ari uses a hadith comparing seeing Allah to the moon to prove literal vision in the afterlife, and how he argues for its reliability through multiple chains.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah like the moon means literal sight, not metaphor.

This passage presents a key proof for the possibility of seeing Allah in the hereafter. Imam al-Ash'ari cites a famous hadith and then argues for its authenticity by noting its widespread transmission, even exceeding accepted traditions. This lesson unpacks both the content and the argumentative strategy.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and analogy
Read the Arabic hadith and its translation. Note the comparison to seeing the moon on a full moon night.
The phrase 'لا تضارون في رؤيته' is important.
2
Understand the literal interpretation
The author argues that because the analogy uses physical sight (رؤية العيان), the vision of Allah must also be physical. Reflect on why this is a strong point.
3
Examine the transmission argument
The author compares the number of narrators of this hadith to other accepted traditions like stoning. See how he uses this to argue that the vision hadith is even more reliable.
Identify the other hadiths mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل آخر :
ومما يدل على إثبات رؤية الله عزّ وجل بالأبصار، ما روته الجماعات من الجهات المختلفة عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : « تَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ كَمَا تَرَوْنَ القَمَرَ لَيْلَةَ البَدْرِ ، لا تَضَارُوْنَ فِي رُؤْيَتِهِ ». والرؤية إذا أطلقت إطلاقاً ومثلت برؤية العيان، لم يكن معناها إلا رؤية العيان .
ورويت الرؤية عن رسول الله من طرق مختلفة عدة رواتها أكثر من عدة خبر الرجم، ومن عدة من روى أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : « لا وَصِيَّةَ لِوارِث » ، ومن عدة رواة المسح على الخفين ومن عدة رواة قول رسول الله : « لا تُنكَحُ المَرْأَةُ عَلَى عَمَّتَّهَا ولا خالتها »، وإذا كان الرجم وما ذكرناه سنناً عند المعتزلة، كانت الرؤية أولى أن تكون سنة ؛ لكثرة رواتها ونقلتها سنةً يرويها خلف عن سلف .
Another proof: Among what indicates the affirmation of seeing Allah Almighty with the eyes is what the groups from various directions narrated from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, that he said: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon, you will not have difficulty in seeing Him.' And the seeing, when used absolutely and compared to seeing with the eye, has no meaning except seeing with the eye. And the vision was narrated from the Messenger of Allah through various paths, the number of its narrators exceeds the number of the report of stoning, and the number of those who narrated that the Prophet said: 'No bequest to an heir', and the number of narrators of wiping over the socks, and the number of narrators of the saying of the Messenger of Allah: 'A woman should not be married to her paternal aunt nor her maternal aunt.' And if stoning and what we have mentioned are sunan according to the Mu'tazila, then the vision is more deserving to be a sunna, due to the abundance of its narrators and transmitters, a sunna transmitted by the later from the earlier.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the possibility of seeing Allah in the hereafter. The author cites a famous hadith in which the Prophet compares seeing Allah to seeing the moon. Since the analogy is to physical sight, the vision must be physical. Then the author argues for the reliability of this hadith by noting that it has been transmitted through numerous chains, far more than other narrations that are accepted as sunna by the Mu'tazila themselves, such as the hadith of stoning, no bequest to an heir, wiping over socks, and prohibition of marrying a woman with her aunt. Therefore, the Mu'tazila should accept this hadith as well.

Key Terms
رؤية بالأبصار
رؤية العيان
خبر الرجم
سنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith comparing seeing Allah to the moon is metaphorical, not literal.
Correction: The author argues that since the analogy is to physical sight (رؤية العيان), the vision of Allah must also be physical, not metaphorical.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the vision hadith is more reliable than the hadith of stoning?
A) Because it is mentioned in the Quran.
B) Because it has more narrators.
C) Because it is in the hadith collections.
D) Because the Mu'tazila accept it.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of seeing Allah like the moon, due to its widespread transmission, proves that believers will see Allah with their eyes in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew with certainty that you will see Allah as clearly as you see the full moon tonight, how would that knowledge change the way you live this day? What would you do differently in your prayers, your thoughts, your hidden moments?
?
The author uses the abundance of narrators to argue that this hadith is trustworthy. Have you let the promise of seeing Allah become as real to you as the most reliably transmitted fact you know? If not, what testimony from your life would you offer as evidence that you truly believe it?
?
You will see your Lord. Does that thought stir longing in you, or hesitation? What is it in your heart that still prefers the comfort of not seeing—what sin, what attachment, what fear—and what are you willing to uproot today so that you can long for that sight without reserve?

Divine Attributes
beginner
4 min
Refuting the Hadith 'Light, How Can I See Him?' as an Objection to Vision
Explains that the hadith 'Nur anna arahu' refers to this world only

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'Light, how can I see Him?' does not negate the vision of Allah in the afterlife, but only applies to this world.

Core Idea
The hadith about light only limits seeing Allah in this world, not the hereafter.

Sanusi now addresses a common objection based on the Prophet's statement 'Light, how can I see Him?' Critics use this hadith to argue that vision of Allah is impossible. However, the author shows the context restricts it to this world, where even created light overwhelms the eye. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author introduces the hadith and immediately states it is not a proof.
The hadith is quoted: 'Light, how can I see Him?'
2
Identify the objection and response
The objection is that the Prophet's words imply vision is impossible. The response is that the hadith specifically refers to seeing Allah in this world, not the afterlife.
The questioner asked about seeing Allah in this world.
3
Understand the analogy of the sun
The author uses the example of the sun: if human eyes cannot even bear looking at the sun (a created light), then they certainly cannot see the Creator in this world without divine strengthening.
This analogy supports the conclusion.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the hadith only negates vision in this world, it does not affect the belief that believers will see Allah in Paradise.
The argument is that the hadith is irrelevant to the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحديث ( أنَّى أَرَاهُ ) لا حجة فيه ؛ لأنه عندما سأل سائل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن رؤية الله عز وجل في الدنيا وقال له هل رأيت ربك؟ فقال: « نُورٌ أَنَّى أَرَاهُ »؟ لأن العين لا تدرك في الدنيا الأنوار المخلوقة على حقائقها؛ لأن الإنسان لو حدق بنظره إلى عين الشمس فأدام النظر إلى عينها لذهب أكثر نور بصره، فإذا كان الله عز وجل حكم في الدنيا بأن لا تقوم العين بالنظر إلى عين الشمس، فأحرى أن لا يثبت البصر للنظر إلى الله عز وجل في الدنيا إلا أن يقويه الله عز وجل
And the hadith 'How can I see Him?' is not a proof; because when a questioner asked the Prophet about seeing Allah Almighty in this world and said to him, 'Have you seen your Lord?' He said: 'Light, how can I see Him?' Because the eye in this world does not perceive created lights as they really are; for if a person stares at the sun and continues looking at it, most of the light of his vision would go. So if Allah Almighty has decreed in this world that the eye cannot bear looking at the sun, then it is even more likely that vision is not affirmed for looking at Allah in this world unless Allah strengthens it.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection against vision of Allah based on the hadith where the Prophet said 'Light, how can I see Him?' The author argues that this hadith refers only to this world. The context makes it clear: the question was about seeing Allah in this life. The Prophet's response indicates that in this world, human eyes cannot even bear looking at created light like the sun, so they certainly cannot see the Creator unless He strengthens them. Therefore, this hadith does not negate vision in the afterlife.

Key Terms
نور
أنى أراه
العين
الدنيا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Light, how can I see Him?' proves that seeing Allah is impossible under all circumstances.
Correction: The hadith only applies to this world, where even created light cannot be fully perceived; it does not negate vision in the hereafter.

Checkpoint
Why does the Prophet's reply 'Light, how can I see Him?' not negate vision in the hereafter according to the author?
A) Because the Prophet did not say it.
B) Because it refers only to this world where eyes cannot bear even created light.
C) Because the question was about seeing the sun.
D) Because the Prophet was speaking metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Light, how can I see Him?' only applies to seeing Allah in this world, not in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet compared seeing Allah in this world to staring at the sun. Do you truly long for the vision of Allah in Paradise, or are you satisfied with temporary worldly sights that fade? What step can you take today to increase that longing?
?
If the greatest created light (the sun) overwhelms your eyes, how will you bear the sight of the Creator unless He strengthens you? Have you ever asked Allah to prepare you for that moment? If not, why not?
?
This hadith is often twisted to deny the vision of Allah altogether. Are there any texts or ideas you have misinterpreted because you did not consider their context? What will you do to ensure you seek proper understanding before forming beliefs?

Consensus of the Companions on Seeing Allah in the Hereafter
Establishes Companion consensus on vision in the hereafter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus (ijma) of the Companions is used to affirm the vision of Allah in the hereafter, even though they differed about seeing Him in this world.

Core Idea
Companion consensus on the hereafter settles the vision debate.

This lesson examines a key argument from classical Sunni theology: the Companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) were unanimous about seeing Allah in the afterlife, even if they disagreed about whether He could be seen in this world. The author uses this reported consensus as a decisive proof, showing that disagreement on one point does not imply disagreement on another. As you read, notice how the argument carefully distinguishes between two different questions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for its rhythm and key terms. Pay attention to the words 'اختلف' (differed) and 'إجماع' (consensus).
The passage is short but packs a logical argument.
2
Identify the two claims
Find where the author states the Companions disagreed about vision in this world and where he states they agreed about vision in the hereafter.
3
Understand the logic of consensus
The author argues that since no Companion denied vision in the hereafter, and many reports affirm it, there is consensus. Ask yourself: why is this argument effective?
Consensus (ijma) is a primary source of Islamic law and theology.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you grasped the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فروية الله سبحانه في الدنيا قد اختلف فيها. وقد روي عن أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن الله عز وجل تراه العيون في الآخرة، وما روي عن أحد منهم أن الله عزّ وجل لا تراه العيون في الآخرة، فلما كانوا على هذا مجمعين وبه قائلين وإن كانوا في رؤيته تعالى في الدنيا مختلفين ، ثبتت الرؤية في الآخرة إجماعاً، وإن كانت في الدنيا مختلفاً فيها، ونحن إنما قصدنا إلى إثبات رؤية الله تعالى في الآخرة .
As for seeing Allah, glory be to Him, in this world, there is disagreement about it. And it has been narrated from the Companions of the Messenger of Allah that Allah will be seen by the eyes in the hereafter, and nothing has been narrated from any of them that Allah will not be seen by the eyes in the hereafter. So since they were unanimous on this and said it, even though they differed about seeing Him in this world, the vision in the hereafter is established by consensus. And we only aim to affirm the vision of Allah in the hereafter.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage argues for the vision of Allah in the hereafter based on the consensus (ijma) of the Companions. The author notes that while there is disagreement among the Companions about whether Allah can be seen in this world, there is no report from any Companion denying vision in the hereafter. In fact, many reports affirm it. Therefore, the Companions were unanimous that believers will see Allah in the afterlife. Since the author's goal is to prove vision in the hereafter, this consensus suffices.

Key Terms
إجماع
آخرة
اختلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreement among early scholars about vision in this world implies disagreement about vision in the hereafter.
Correction: The Companions differed about seeing Allah in this world but were unanimous about seeing Him in the hereafter.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for affirming vision of Allah in the hereafter according to this passage?
A) The consensus of the Companions.
B) The Quranic verses.
C) The hadith about the moon.
D) The disagreement about vision in this world.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' consensus that Allah will be seen in the hereafter is a decisive proof, notwithstanding their disagreement about seeing Him in this world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Companions could disagree on seeing Allah in this world yet unanimously affirm it in the hereafter, what does that teach you about holding certainty in matters where your own understanding may differ from the consensus of the righteous predecessors? Are you willing to submit to their collective judgment even when your own reasoning might suggest otherwise?
?
Consider an area in your deen where you have doubts or confusion. How does the example of the Companions' consensus on ru'yat Allah in the hereafter encourage you to seek out the established Sunnah and jama'ah, rather than rely on your own limited perspective?
?
The passage shows that absence of denial is treated as evidence of agreement. Is there an aspect of Islamic teaching that you have been ignoring or marginalizing because you haven't heard a strong objection to it from a respected source? How will you now actively seek to affirm what the righteous have affirmed, even if it challenges your preferences?
