Establishing Ru'ya: Analogy, Hadith Refutation, and Consensus
Al-Ash'ari argues for afterlife vision via analogy, hadith interpretation, and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's two-part argument for the vision of Allah in the afterlife: the analogy of sunna and the refutation of the 'How can I see Him?' hadith, culminating in the consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
The Companions' consensus on afterlife vision is decisive, outweighing disputed earthly vision.

In this passage, al-Ash'ari defends the Sunni doctrine that believers will see Allah in the afterlife. He uses a clever analogy to counter Mu'tazili skepticism and refutes a key hadith often cited against ru'ya. Finally, he grounds his argument in the unanimous agreement of the Prophet's Companions—a powerful proof in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Follow the analogy of sunna
Notice how al-Ash'ari compares the hadiths on ru'ya to other accepted sunna practices (like stoning and marriage prohibitions) that the Mu'tazila themselves accept. He argues that ru'ya hadiths have even more narrators.
This is a rhetorical move to show consistency.
2
Understand the refutation of 'anna arahu'
Read how al-Ash'ari explains that the Prophet's reply 'Light, how can I see Him?' refers only to this world, because the eye cannot bear even created light like the sun, let alone the divine light.
The hadith does not deny afterlife vision.
3
Grasp the consensus argument
Al-Ash'ari states that all Companions affirmed ru'ya in the afterlife, with no dissenting report. This consensus (ijma') establishes the doctrine beyond doubt.
Consensus is a primary source of Islamic law and theology.
4
See the final rhetorical twist
Al-Ash'ari notes that the hadith they cite actually argues against the Mu'tazila because they deny that Allah is light in reality.
This undermines their own position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المَرْأَةُ عَلَى عَمَّتَهَا ولا خالتها (۱) ، وإذا كان الرجم وما ذكرناه سنناً عند المعتزلة، كانت الرؤية أولى أن تكون سنة ؛ لكثرة رواتها ونقلتها سنةً يرويها خلف عن سلف . وحديث ( أنَّى أَرَاهُ (۲) لا حجة فيه ؛ لأنه عندما سأل سائل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ، عن رؤية الله عز وجل في الدنيا وقال له هل رأيت ربك؟ فقال: « نُورٌ أَنَّى أَرَاهُ »؟ لأن العين لا تدرك في الدنيا الأنوار المخلوقة على حقائقها؛ لأن الإنسان لو حدق بنظره إلى عين الشمس فأدام النظر إلى عينها لذهب أكثر نور بصره، فإذا كان الله عز وجل حكم في الدنيا بأن لا تقـوم الـعـيـن بـالنظر إلى عين الشمس، فأحرى أن لا يثبت البصر للنظر إلى الله عز وجل في الدنيا إلا أن يقويه الله عز وجل فرؤية الله سبحانه في الدنيا قد اختلف فيها. وقد روي عن أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن الله عز وجل تراه العيون في الآخرة، وما روي عن أحد منهم أن الله عزّ وجل لا تراه العيون في الآخرة، فلما كانوا على هذا مجمعين وبه قائلين وإن كانوا في رؤيته تعالى في الدنيا مختلفين ، ثبتت الرؤية في الآخرة إجماعاً، وإن كانت في الدنيا مختلفاً فيها، ونحن إنما قصدنا إلى إثبات رؤية الله تعالى في الآخرة . على أن هذه الرواية على المعتزلة لا لهم ؛ لأنهم ينكرون أن الله نور في الحقيقة. فإذا احتجوا بخبر هم له تاركون وعنه منحرفون كانوا محجوجين
The woman [is not to be married] with her paternal aunt nor her maternal aunt (1), and when stoning and what we mentioned are sunna according to the Mu'tazila, then the vision (ru'ya) is more deserving to be sunna, due to the abundance of its narrators and transmitters, a sunna transmitted by later generations from earlier ones. And the hadith 'How can I see Him?' (2) is no proof; because when a questioner asked the Prophet, peace be upon him, about seeing Allah, Mighty and Majestic, in this world and said to him, 'Did you see your Lord?' he said, 'Light, how can I see Him?' because the eye in this world does not perceive created lights in their reality; for if a man were to stare at the sun and continue looking at its eye, most of the light of his vision would go. So if Allah, Mighty and Majestic, decreed in this world that the eye cannot withstand looking at the sun's eye, then it is more fitting that sight is not established for looking at Allah, Mighty and Majestic, in this world unless Allah strengthens him. So seeing Allah in this world has been disputed. And it has been narrated from the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, that the eyes see Allah in the afterlife, and nothing has been narrated from any of them that the eyes do not see Allah in the afterlife. So since they were in consensus on this and expressed it, even if they differed about seeing Him in this world, the vision in the afterlife is established by consensus, even if it is disputed in this world, and we only intend to establish the vision of Allah, Exalted, in the afterlife. Moreover, this narration is against the Mu'tazila, not for them; because they deny that Allah is light in reality. So if they argue with a report which they themselves abandon and deviate from, they are refuted.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage contains a two-part argument for the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife. First, al-Ash'ari draws an analogy: if the Mu'tazila accept as sunna certain hadiths (like the prohibition of marrying a woman with her aunt), then the hadiths on ru'ya are even more worthy because they have more narrators. Second, he addresses the hadith 'anna arahu' ('How can I see Him?'), explaining that it refers only to this world—the eye cannot bear created lights, let alone the divine light. He notes that the Companions unanimously affirmed seeing Allah in the afterlife, even if they differed about seeing Him in this world. This consensus (ijma') is decisive. Finally, he points out that the same hadith actually argues against the Mu'tazila because they deny that Allah is light, so they cannot use it as evidence.

Key Terms
رؤية
سنة
إجماع
نور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari, why is the vision of Allah in the afterlife established?
A) Because the Mu'tazila accept stoning as sunna
B) Because the Companions had consensus on it
C) Because the hadith 'anna arahu' proves it
D) Because vision in this world is also established

Key Takeaway
The vision of Allah in the afterlife is firmly established by the consensus of the Companions, and the hadith 'How can I see Him?' only applies to this world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself distracted by doubts about Allah's visibility in the afterlife, even though the Companions were unanimous? What is in your heart that resists this truth? This week, reflect on how longing to see Allah can transform your worship.
?
When you hear the hadith 'Light, how can I see Him?' do you feel a temptation to reduce Allah to something limited? Repent of any anthropomorphic thoughts and affirm His majesty.
?
If the Companions could agree on this crucial matter despite their differences on earthly vision, what matters of faith do you need to align with them, even when you struggle to fully understand? Commit to learning one more article of faith this week.

Another Proof for Ru'ya: Existence Implies Visibility
Al-Ash'ari argues that anything existent can be seen, so Allah can be seen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's argument that because Allah exists, He can be seen, and why denying ru'ya leads to ta'til.

Core Idea
Existence itself is the basis for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Al-Ash'ari presents a concise logical proof: everything existent can be shown by Allah, so since Allah exists, seeing Him is possible. He then exposes the hidden agenda of those who deny ru'ya—they aim to strip Allah of all attributes (ta'til). This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: 'there is no existent thing except that it is permissible...'
2
Identify the major premise
The argument's key premise: all existents can be seen. Only the non-existent cannot. Confirm this in the text.
This is a general principle about visibility.
3
Understand the motive behind denial
Al-Ash'ari claims that those who deny ru'ya actually aim at ta'til (stripping Allah of attributes). Reflect on why this accusation is serious.
Ta'til is considered a form of disbelief in Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل آخر : ومما يدل على رؤية الله عز وجل بالأبصار؛ أنه ليس موجود إلا وجائز أن يريناه الله عز وجل، وإنما لا يجوز أن يُرى المعدوم، فلما كان الله عز وجل موجوداً مثبتاً كان غير مستحيل أن يرينا نفسه عزَّ وجلّ . وإنما أراد مَنْ نفى رؤية الله عز وجل بالأبصار التعطيل فلما لم يمكنهم أن يظهروا التعطيل صراحاً أظهروا ما يؤول بهم إلى التعطيل والجحود، تعالى الله عن ذلك علواً كبيراً .
Another proof: Among what indicates the vision of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, by the eyes is that there is no existent thing except that it is permissible for Allah, Mighty and Majestic, to show it to us, and only the non-existent cannot be seen. So since Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is an established existent, it is not impossible for Him to show us Himself, Mighty and Majestic. Those who deny the vision of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, by the eyes only intend nullification (ta'til). So when they could not openly display nullification, they displayed what leads them to nullification and denial. Exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a proof for ru'ya based on the general principle that whatever exists can potentially be seen by God's power. The only barrier to being seen is non-existence. Since Allah exists, it is not impossible for Him to show Himself. Al-Ash'ari then diagnoses the motive of those who deny ru'ya: they aim at ta'til (stripping Allah of His attributes). Unable to openly deny attributes, they deny ru'ya as a step toward complete denial. This unmasks their real intention.

Key Terms
رؤية بالأبصار
موجود
معدوم
تعطيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ru'ya is impossible because Allah is not a body, so He cannot be seen.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari's argument does not depend on corporeality; it is based on existence. Anything existent can be shown by Allah, regardless of being a body or not.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari say is the only thing that cannot be seen?
A) The non-existent
B) The existent
C) Bodies
D) Lights

Key Takeaway
The existence of Allah is sufficient to establish the possibility of seeing Him; denial of ru'ya is a step toward denying all attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage says that denying the vision of Allah is a step toward nullifying all His attributes. Honestly examine your heart: do you ever find yourself uncomfortable with certain attributes of Allah—like His all-encompassing knowledge or His absolute justice—and wish they were less real? What small denial might you be permitting that could lead to a larger rejection? Commit today to affirming even the attributes that challenge you.
?
Al-Ash'ari argues that existence itself makes vision possible. How does your daily life reflect the reality that Allah is the most existent, most real Being? If you truly lived as if He exists and can be seen, what specific change would you make in your prayer, your speech, or your thoughts before this day ends?
?
The argument is simple: every existent can be seen. But do you treat Allah as merely a concept, or as a living, seeing Reality? When you say 'Allah exists,' does it carry the same weight as when you say 'this desk exists'—or is it more? Ask yourself: if you were to see Allah tomorrow, would you be overwhelmed with joy or with fear? What does your answer reveal about your relationship with Him?

Proof of Ru'ya from Allah's Seeing All Things
Al-Ash'ari argues from Allah's seeing to the possibility of being seen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's logical argument that Allah's seeing all things implies that He can be seen.

Core Idea
If Allah sees all things, He must see Himself, and thus can be seen.

Al-Ash'ari presents a fascinating proof based on the logical correlation between seeing and being seen. He argues that the very ability to see implies the possibility of being seen, using a parallel with knowledge. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let the flow of the argument settle in your mind.
Notice the parallel structure between sight and knowledge.
2
Identify the key analogy
Al-Ash'ari draws a parallel between knowledge and sight. Find how he uses the analogy to argue from Allah's knowing Himself to His making Himself known.
3
Trace the logical progression
Follow the steps: Allah sees things → one who doesn't see self cannot see things → Allah sees self → it is permissible for Him to show us Himself.
This is the core logical chain.
4
Examine the scriptural support
Al-Ash'ari cites Quran 20:46 where Allah says 'I hear and see.' Understand how this verse supports the proof.
The verse shows Allah sees His creation.
5
Reflect on the reductio argument
Consider the final statement: denying ru'ya would require denying Allah's attributes of sight, knowledge, and power. Why is that an absurd consequence?
Think about the necessary link between attributes and their effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸۲- دليل آخر : ومما يدل على رؤية الله سبحانه بالأبصار، أن الله عز وجل يرى الأشياء، وإذا كان للأشياء رائياً فلا يرى الأشياء من لا يرى نفسه، وإذا كان لنفسه رائياً فجائز أن يرينا نفسه، وذلك أن من لا يعلم نفسه لا يعلم شيئاً، فلما كان الله عز وجل عالماً بالأشياء كان عالماً بنفسه، فلذلك من لا يرى نفسه لا يرى الأشياء، ولما كان الله عز وجل رائياً للأشياء كان رائياً لنفسه، وإذا كان رائياً لها فجائز أن يرينا نفسه كما أنه لما كان عالماً بنفسه جاز أن يعلمناها وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّنِي مَعَكُمَا أَسْمَعُ وَأرى ) [ طه : ٤٦] فأخبر أنه سمع كلامهما ورآهما . ومن زعم أن الله عز وجل لا يجوز أن يُرى بالأبصار يلزمه أن لا يجوز أن يكون الله عز وجل رائياً، ولا عالماً ولا قادراً؛ لأن العالم القادر الرائي جائز أن يُرَى
82- Another proof: Among what indicates the vision of Allah, Glorified, by the eyes is that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, sees things. And if He is a seer of things, then one who does not see himself does not see things. And if He is a seer of Himself, then it is permissible for Him to show us Himself. This is because one who does not know himself knows nothing. Since Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is knowledgeable of things, He is knowledgeable of Himself. Therefore, one who does not see himself does not see things. And since Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is a seer of things, He is a seer of Himself. And if He is a seer of it, then it is permissible for Him to show us Himself, just as when He is knowledgeable of Himself, it is permissible for Him to make us know it. And Allah, Exalted, said: 'Indeed, I am with you, I hear and see' [Ta-Ha 20:46]. So He informed that He heard their speech and saw them. And whoever claims that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, cannot be seen by the eyes, it follows that He cannot be a seer, nor knowledgeable, nor powerful; because the knowledgeable, powerful seer can be seen.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the attribute of seeing (ru'ya) itself. Al-Ash'ari argues that someone who cannot see himself cannot see others. Since Allah sees all things, He must see Himself. Therefore, it is possible for Him to be seen by others, because the very ability to see implies the possibility of being seen (as a logical correlation). He draws a parallel with knowledge: Allah knows Himself, so He can make Himself known. He supports this with the verse where Allah says 'I hear and see,' showing that He sees His creation. Finally, he uses a reductio: if one denies that Allah can be seen, then one must also deny that He sees, knows, and is powerful, because the attributes of seeing, knowing, and power entail the possibility of being seen, known, and influenced. This is absurd, so ru'ya is affirmed.

Key Terms
رائٍ
رؤية
علم
قدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing Allah would require Allah to have a body.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari argues from the logical correlation between seeing and being seen, not from physicality. The possibility of being seen is entailed by the attribute of sight itself, regardless of corporeality.

Checkpoint
What does al-Ash'ari argue about someone who does not see himself?
A) He cannot see others
B) He can still see others
C) He is not knowledgeable

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari proves the possibility of seeing Allah by showing that Allah's perfect sight of all things logically entails the possibility of being seen.

?
Al-Ash'ari argues that denying the possibility of seeing Allah leads to denying His perfection. Will you submit to what the Qur'an and reason demand, even if your mind struggles?

First Proof: Seeing God is Possible Because He Exists
Al-Ash'ari argues for the possibility of seeing God based on His existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's logical argument that seeing God is possible because He exists, and why opponents' denial leads to ta'til.

Core Idea
Only non-existent things cannot be seen; God exists, so He can be seen.

Al-Ash'ari presents a concise logical proof for the possibility of seeing God with the eyes in the afterlife. He establishes a universal principle: anything that exists can potentially be shown to us by God. Since God is undeniably existent, there is no rational barrier to Him granting vision of Himself. This passage also exposes the deeper motive of those who deny divine vision—they are aiming for ta'til, the negation of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the simple logical structure of the argument.
Notice the cause-and-effect reasoning.
2
Identify the key premise
Look for the principle that the non-existent cannot be seen, and that all existent things are potentially seeable. Confirm that this premise applies to God.
The proof hinges on existence vs. non-existence.
3
Understand the motive of opponents
Reflect on why Al-Ash'ari mentions the opponents' hidden goal of ta'til (negation of attributes). How does denying vision serve that goal?
He exposes that their claim leads to juhud (denial).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل آخر : ومما يدل على رؤية الله عز وجل بالأبصار ؛ أنه ليس موجود إلا وجائز أن يريناه الله عز وجل، وإنما لا يجوز أن يُرى المعدوم، فلما كان الله عز وجل موجوداً مثبتاً كان غير مستحيل أن يرينا نفسه عزَّ وجلّ . وإنما أراد مَنْ نفى رؤية الله عز وجل بالأبصار التعطيل فلما لم يمكنهم أن يظهروا التعطيل صراحاً أظهروا ما يؤول بهم إلى التعطيل والجحود، تعالى الله عن ذلك علواً كبيراً .
Another proof: And among what indicates the seeing of Allah Almighty with eyes is that there is no existent thing except that it is possible for Allah Almighty to show it to us, and indeed it is not permissible to see the non-existent. So since Allah Almighty is an established existent, it is not impossible for Him to show us Himself, Mighty and Majestic. And those who denied the seeing of Allah Almighty with eyes only intended nullification (ta'til). So when they were unable to show nullification openly, they showed what leads them to nullification and denial. Exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility of seeing God with the eyes in the afterlife. Al-Ash'ari begins by stating the premise: anything that exists can potentially be shown to us by God. The only thing that is impossible to see is something that is non-existent (ma'dum). Since God is an established existent (mawjud muthbat), it is not inherently impossible for Him to make Himself seen. The author then exposes the real motive of those who deny vision: they aim for ta'til (negation of God's attributes or actions). Unable to openly deny God, they resort to claiming that seeing God is impossible, which leads to juhud (denial). Al-Ash'ari ends by exalting Allah above such claims.

Second Proof: God Sees Himself So He Can Show Himself
Al-Ash'ari argues by analogy that since God sees things, He sees Himself and can show Himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's logical argument that God's ability to see things implies He sees Himself and can therefore show Himself to others.

Core Idea
If God sees all things, then He sees Himself, and can show Himself.

Al-Ash'ari presents a second proof for the possibility of seeing God with eyes. He builds on a simple logical principle: if you see things, you must also see yourself. Applying this to God, who sees all things, leads to the conclusion that He sees Himself and can thus show Himself. The argument is reinforced by a Qur'anic verse and a reductio against the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the flow of Al-Ash'ari's reasoning.
Notice the use of analogy between knowledge and vision.
2
Identify the main premise
Find the key claim: 'One who does not see himself cannot see things.' This is the logical foundation.
3
Apply the premise to God
Since God sees all things, He must see Himself. Then it becomes possible for Him to show Himself to others.
This parallels the knowledge attribute: He knows Himself and taught us about Himself.
4
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
Read the verse from Surah Ta-Ha where Allah says He hears and sees Moses and Aaron. This supports that God sees.
5
Understand the reductio
Al-Ash'ari argues that denying the possibility of being seen leads to denying that God is a seer, knower, or powerful, which is absurd.
This shows the opponent's position is inconsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸۲- دليل آخر : ومما يدل على رؤية الله سبحانه بالأبصار، أن الله عز وجل يرى الأشياء، وإذا كان للأشياء رائياً فلا يرى الأشياء من لا يرى نفسه، وإذا كان لنفسه رائياً فجائز أن يرينا نفسه، وذلك أن من لا يعلم نفسه لا يعلم شيئاً، فلما كان الله عز وجل عالماً بالأشياء كان عالماً بنفسه، فلذلك من لا يرى نفسه لا يرى الأشياء، ولما كان الله عز وجل رائياً للأشياء كان رائياً لنفسه، وإذا كان رائياً لها فجائز أن يرينا نفسه كما أنه لما كان عالماً بنفسه جاز أن يعلمناها وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّنِي مَعَكُمَا أَسْمَعُ وَأرى ) [ طه : ٤٦] فأخبر أنه سمع كلامهما ورآهما . ومن زعم أن الله عز وجل لا يجوز أن يُرى بالأبصار يلزمه أن لا يجوز أن يكون الله عز وجل رائياً، ولا عالماً ولا قادراً؛ لأن العالم القادر الرائي جائز أن يُرَى
82. Another proof: And among what indicates the seeing of Allah, may He be glorified, with eyes, is that Allah Almighty sees things. And if He is a seer of things, then one who does not see himself cannot see things. And if He is a seer of Himself, then it is possible that He show us Himself. That is because one who does not know himself does not know anything. So since Allah Almighty is knower of things, He is knower of Himself. Therefore, one who does not see himself does not see things. And since Allah Almighty is a seer of things, He is a seer of Himself. And if He is a seer of them, then He can show us Himself, just as He was a knower of Himself, so He enabled us to know Him. And Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, I am with you; I hear and see' [Ta-Ha 20:46], so He informed that He heard their speech and saw them. And whoever claims that Allah Almighty cannot be seen by eyes must necessarily claim that Allah Almighty cannot be a seer, nor a knower, nor powerful; because the knower, powerful, seer may be seen.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage contains a second proof for the possibility of seeing God. Al-Ash'ari argues analogically: just as knowledge of things requires self-knowledge, seeing things requires self-seeing. Since God knows all things, He knows Himself; since He sees all things, He sees Himself. Therefore, it is possible for Him to show Himself to others, just as He made Himself known. He cites the Qur'anic verse where Allah tells Moses and Aaron that He hears and sees them. Al-Ash'ari then uses a reductio ad absurdum: anyone who denies that God can be seen must also deny that God is a seer, knower, or powerful, because the attributes of being a knower, powerful, and seer imply the possibility of being seen. This shows the inconsistency of the opponent.

Correction: Al-Ash'ari is using a logical analogy based on attributes, not anthropomorphism. He argues from the logical consistency of attributes, not from physical sight.

The Real Meaning of 'Seeing' God: Refuting the Knowledge Interpretation
Al-Ash'ari refutes the claim that the hadith about seeing God means knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Ash'ari rejects the interpretation that 'seeing God' in the hadith refers only to knowledge, and how he uses the concepts of glad tidings and generality to defend literal vision.

Core Idea
Seeing God is a unique blessing for believers; it cannot be mere knowledge.

In this passage, Al-Ash'ari addresses a common objection: that the Prophet's statement 'You will see your Lord' actually means 'you will know your Lord.' He refutes this by pointing out that glad tidings must be exclusive to believers, and that the Prophet's words are general, covering both physical and spiritual sight. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Al-Ash'ari presents a clear objection and then gives his refutation.
The objector claims 'seeing' means 'knowing necessarily'.
2
Understand the 'glad tiding' argument
Al-Ash'ari argues that the Prophet gave this as a glad tiding (bishara) to his companions. If it meant mere knowledge, disbelievers also have knowledge, so it wouldn't be exclusive.
Glad tidings must be something special for believers.
3
Understand the 'generality' argument
The Prophet's words 'You will see your Lord' are general, not restricted to one type of seeing. They include both physical eye-sight and heart-sight.
This refutes the attempt to limit it to knowledge.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why this matters: literal vision of God in the afterlife is a key Sunni doctrine, affirming God's visible nature without likening Him to creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸۳- مسألة : فإن قال قائل : قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : ( تَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُم ) يعني تعلمون ربكم اضطراراً . قيل له : إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لأصحابه هذا على سبيل البشارة، فقال: فَكَيْفَ بِكُمْ إِذَا رَأَيْتُمُ الله عَزَّ وَجَلَّ، ولا يجوز أن يبشرهم بأمر يَشركهم فيه الكفار ، على أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: « تَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ » وليس يعني رؤية دون رؤية، بل ذلك عام في رؤية العين ورؤية القلب
83. Question: If someone says: The saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, 'You will see your Lord' means you will know your Lord necessarily. It is said to him: The Prophet, peace be upon him, said this to his companions as a glad tiding, and said: 'How will you be when you see Allah Almighty?' And it is not permissible that he give them glad tidings of something in which the disbelievers share with them. In addition, the Prophet, peace be upon him, said 'You will see your Lord' and he did not mean a seeing to the exclusion of another seeing; rather that is general for the seeing of the eye and the seeing of the heart.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (mas'ala). The objector claims that the hadith 'You will see your Lord' actually means 'you will know your Lord necessarily' (i.e., a cognitive vision, not literal sight). Al-Ash'ari refutes this with two points. First, the Prophet gave this statement as a glad tiding (bishara). If it meant only knowledge, then disbelievers also have knowledge of God, so the glad tiding would not be exclusive to believers. Second, the Prophet's wording is general ('amm), encompassing both physical sight (ru'yat al-'ayn) and spiritual insight (ru'yat al-qalb). It does not restrict to one type. Thus the interpretation as mere knowledge is invalid.

Misconception: Some claim 'seeing' in the afterlife is metaphorical and means only knowledge.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari insists it is literal and includes both physical and spiritual dimensions, as the Prophet's words are general and the glad tiding must be exclusive to believers.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari defends the literal vision of God by showing that the hadith is a glad tiding exclusive to believers and uses general wording that includes both physical and inner sight.

Third Proof: Seeing God is the Greatest Bliss
Al-Ash'ari argues from consensus on paradise's delights that seeing God is the best.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's third proof that seeing Allah in Paradise is the greatest bliss, how he uses consensus and a fortiori reasoning, and why this does not imply anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is the supreme delight of Paradise, proven by consensus and reason.

Al-Ash'ari now presents a third proof for the reality of seeing Allah in the afterlife. Building on the consensus that Paradise contains unimaginable joys, he argues that the greatest of these is the vision of Allah Himself. This passage combines scriptural allusion, logical reasoning, and a defense against the charge of anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage aloud or silently, then follow the literal translation. Notice how Al-Ash'ari structures his argument.
The hadith 'no eye has seen' is a famous description of Paradise.
2
Identify the consensus premise
Find where Al-Ash'ari states the agreement of Muslims about Paradise's delights. This is his starting point.
3
Trace the a fortiori argument
Al-Ash'ari argues that if seeing the Prophet is the best pleasure, then seeing Allah is even better. Follow this logic step by step.
This is a qiyas awlawi (a fortiori) argument.
4
Understand the objection and reply
The passage ends by addressing a potential problem: that vision might imply change in God. Al-Ash'ari explains why it does not.
The key point: vision is an act of the seer, not an effect on the seen.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Al-Ash'ari insists that Allah can show Himself without making Him a body or changing Him. This refutes anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸٤- دليل آخر : إن المسلمين اتفقوا على أن الجنة فيها ما لا عين رأت، ولا أذن سمعت، ولا خطر على قلب بشر من العيش السليم والنعيم المقيم وليس نعيم في الجنة أفضل من رؤية الله عز وجل بالأبصار، وأكثر من عبد الله عز وجل عبده للنظر إلى وجهه الكريم - أرانا الله إياه بفضله ـ فإذا لم يكن بعد رؤية الله أفضل من رؤية نبيه ، وكانت رؤية نبي الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أفضل لذات الجنة، كانت رؤية الله عز وجل أفضل من رؤية نبيه ، وإذا كان ذلك كذلك لم يحرم الله أنبياءه المرسلين، وملائكته المقربين، وجماعة المؤمنين والصديقين النظر إلى وجهه عز وجل . وذلك أن الرؤية لا تؤثر في المرئي ؛ لأن رؤية الرائي تقوم به، فإذا كان هذا هكذا، وكانت الرؤية غير مؤثرة في المرئي لم توجب تشبيهاً ولا انقلاباً عن حقيقة، ولم يستحل على الله عز وجل أن يُري عباده المؤمنين نفسه في جنانه
84. Another proof: The Muslims agree that Paradise contains what no eye has seen, no ear has heard, and has not occurred to the heart of any human being, of secure life and everlasting bliss. And there is no bliss in Paradise better than seeing Allah Almighty with eyes. And most of those who worshipped Allah Almighty worshipped Him for the sake of looking at His noble face — may Allah show it to us by His grace. So if there is nothing after seeing Allah that is better than seeing His Prophet, and the seeing of the Prophet of Allah, peace be upon him, is the best of the pleasures of Paradise, then seeing Allah Almighty is better than seeing His Prophet. And if that is so, Allah would not deprive His sent prophets, His near angels, and the group of believers and truthful ones from looking at His face, Mighty and Majestic. That is because vision does not affect the seen; for the vision of the seer subsists in him. So if this is the case, and vision is not affecting the seen, it does not necessitate resemblance (tashbih) nor change from reality, and it is not impossible for Allah Almighty to show Himself to His believing servants in His Paradise.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a third proof for the reality of seeing God in Paradise. Al-Ash'ari begins with a consensus (ijma'): Muslims agree that Paradise contains inconceivable delights. Among these, the greatest delight is seeing God. Most worshippers seek the vision of God's face. He then argues a fortiori: if seeing God is greater than seeing the Prophet, and seeing the Prophet is the highest pleasure, then God would not deprive His prophets, angels, and believers of that pleasure. He then addresses a potential objection: vision does not affect the object seen; it is an act of the seer. Therefore, seeing God does not imply tashbih (anthropomorphism) or change in God's essence. Hence, it is not impossible for God to grant this vision in Paradise.

Key Terms
إجماع
نعيم الجنة
رؤية الله
تشبيه
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that seeing God would imply God is a body or changes.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari clarifies that vision is an act of the seer and does not affect the seen, so no tashbih or change occurs.

Checkpoint

A) Eating and drinking.
B) Seeing the Prophet.
C) Seeing Allah.
D) Living eternally.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari proves the beatific vision by consensus and a fortiori reasoning, while refuting anthropomorphism by showing vision does not affect the seen.

The proof shows that vision does not affect the seen. Are you afraid that drawing close to Allah will somehow diminish His majesty or change your relationship? What assumptions about God's transcendence have prevented you from yearning for His intimate presence?

Another Proof for the Vision of Allah: Superiority and Clarity
Ash'ari presents a proof for ru'ya based on superiority and refutes tashbīh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ash'ari's proof for the vision of Allah based on the superiority of seeing Allah over seeing the Prophet, and why this vision does not imply anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is the greatest bliss, and since it does not affect Him, it is possible without tashbīh.

Imam al-Ash'ari presents another compelling argument for the beatific vision in the afterlife. He addresses an objection that the Prophet's words were mere glad tidings, then builds a logical case that the vision of Allah is superior to all other delights, including that of the Prophet. Finally, he clarifies that vision does not necessitate change or likeness in the seen, thus preserving Allah's transcendence. Let's examine this proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ash'ari uses a combination of hadith and rational argument.
2
Identify the three parts
The passage has three main sections: rebuttal of an objection, a superiority argument, and a clarification about vision not affecting the seen. Find where each begins and ends.
The objection is that the Prophet's statement was only figurative glad tidings.
3
Understand the superiority hierarchy
Ash'ari argues that the vision of Allah is the greatest delight in Paradise, and the vision of the Prophet is the best of created delights. Therefore, the vision of Allah is superior. Ask yourself why this hierarchy proves the vision of Allah.
This reasoning is based on the principle that if the lesser is granted, the greater is not withheld.
4
Grasp the philosophical point
Ash'ari states that vision does not affect the seen; it subsists in the seer. Therefore, seeing Allah does not imply a change in Allah or likening Him to created things. Understand why this is crucial for defending orthodox belief.
This addresses the Mu'tazilite objection that ru'ya necessitates tashbīh.
5
Reflect on the overall proof
Consider how this proof complements other arguments for ru'ya, such as those from the Qur'an and consensus. How does Ash'ari combine scriptural and rational reasoning?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨٤- دليل آخر : قيل له : إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لأصحابه هذا على سبيل البشارة، فقال: «فكيف بكم إذا رأيتم الله عز وجل»، ولا يجوز أن يبشرهم بأمر يشركهم فيه الكفار، على أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «ترون ربكم» وليس يعني رؤية دون رؤية، بل ذلك عام في رؤية العين ورؤية القلب.

وذلك أن المسلمين اتفقوا على أن الجنة فيها ما لا عين رأت، ولا أذن سمعت، ولا خطر على قلب بشر من العيش السليم والنعيم المقيم وليس نعيم في الجنة أفضل من رؤية الله عز وجل بالأبصار، وأكثر من عبد الله عز وجل عبده للنظر إلى وجهه الكريم - أرانا الله إياه بفضله - فإذا لم يكن بعد رؤية الله أفضل من رؤية نبيه، وكانت رؤية نبي الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أفضل لذات الجنة، كانت رؤية الله عز وجل أفضل من رؤية نبيه، وإذا كان ذلك كذلك لم يحرم الله أنبياءه المرسلين، وملائكته المقربين، وجماعة المؤمنين والصديقين النظر إلى وجهه عز وجل.

وذلك أن الرؤية لا تؤثر في المرئي؛ لأن رؤية الرائي تقوم به، فإذا كان هذا هكذا، وكانت الرؤية غير مؤثرة في المرئي لم توجب تشبيهاً ولا انقلاباً عن حقيقة، ولم يستحل على الله عز وجل أن يري عباده المؤمنين نفسه في جنانه.
84 - Another proof: It was said to him: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said to his companions this by way of glad tidings, saying: 'How will it be for you when you see Allah, the Mighty and Majestic?' And it is not permissible that he gives them glad tidings of something in which the disbelievers share with them. Moreover, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'You will see your Lord,' and he did not mean a vision without a vision, rather that is general concerning vision of the eye and vision of the heart.

And that is because Muslims are agreed that in Paradise there is what no eye has seen, no ear has heard, and has not occurred to the heart of any human of pure life and everlasting bliss, and there is no bliss in Paradise better than the vision of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, with the eyes. And most of those who worship Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, worship Him for the sake of looking upon His Noble Face – may Allah show us it by His grace – so if after the vision of Allah there is nothing better than the vision of His Prophet, and the vision of the Prophet of Allah (peace be upon him) is the best of the delights of Paradise, then the vision of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, is better than the vision of His Prophet. And if that is so, Allah would not deprive His sent prophets, His close angels, and the group of believers and the truthful of looking upon His Face, the Mighty and Majestic.

And that is because vision does not affect the seen; for the vision of the seer subsists in him. So if this is the case, and vision does not affect the seen, it does not necessitate likeness (tashbīh) nor a change from reality, and it is not impossible for Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, to show His believing servants Himself in His Paradise.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the vision of Allah (ru'ya) in the afterlife. The author begins by addressing an objection: some claim that the Prophet's statement to his companions was merely glad tidings, not a literal promise. Ash'ari rebuts that if it were only for believers, disbelievers would not share it, yet the vision is exclusive to believers. He then cites the hadith 'You will see your Lord' and argues it is general for both physical and heart's vision. Next, he uses a logical superiority argument: the greatest bliss in Paradise is the vision of Allah, and it is better than the vision of the Prophet. Since Allah grants the Prophet's vision to believers, He would not withhold His own vision, especially from the prophets and angels. Finally, he addresses the theological concern of anthropomorphism: vision does not affect the seen (al-mar'ī), so it does not imply that Allah changes or becomes like created things. Thus, it is possible for Allah to show Himself to believers in Paradise without compromising His transcendence. This proof combines scriptural, rational, and theological reasoning.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'ya)
أبصار (absār)
تشبيه (tashbīh)
الجنة (al-janna)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The vision of Allah implies anthropomorphism (tashbīh) because seeing necessarily involves resemblance.
Correction: Ash'ari explains that vision does not affect the seen; it is an attribute of the seer. Therefore, seeing Allah does not necessitate any likeness or change in Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Ash'ari, why is the vision of Allah better than the vision of the Prophet?
A) Because the Prophet himself preferred it.
B) Because it is the greatest delight in Paradise, and the vision of the Prophet is a lesser delight.
C) Because the disbelievers can also see the Prophet but not Allah.
D) Because Allah is more beautiful than the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari proves that Allah can be seen in Paradise without tashbīh because vision does not affect the seen, and the superiority of this vision over all other delights ensures its bestowal upon believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the greatest bliss is seeing Allah, what does your current pursuit of pleasures say about your longing for that vision? Are you truly living as if that is your goal? What needs to change in your priorities today?
?
The passage says 'most of those who worship Allah worship Him for the sake of looking upon His Noble Face.' Is that your motivation in worship? If not, what is? How can you cultivate that longing?
?
The proof shows that vision does not affect Allah. Do you sometimes think your worship or lack thereof affects Allah? How does understanding His transcendence change your humility and hope?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Argument on Vision of Allah
Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazila use of verse 6:103 to deny the vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazila interpretation of verse 6:103 by analyzing the Arabic term 'absār' and exposing a logical inconsistency in their argument.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's proof collapses when 'absār' is understood to include both physical and spiritual sight.

Imam Ash'ari presents and then refutes the Mu'tazila argument against the vision of Allah. The Mu'tazila cite Quran 6:103, claiming that 'eyesight does not perceive Him' applies universally. Ash'ari responds by examining the Arabic word 'absār', showing that it can refer to both physical eyes and inner insight. He then demonstrates that the Mu'tazila cannot consistently apply their own reasoning, turning their argument against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Begin by reading the passage where Ash'ari presents the Mu'tazila proof. Notice how they use the verse and the principle of generality to deny vision.
The Mu'tazila claim that since 'He perceives eyesight' is general, the negation must also be general.
2
Analyze the word 'absār'
Ash'ari then explains that 'absār' can mean both physical eyes and the sight of the heart. Look at the Quranic examples and linguistic usage he provides.
This is the key move in the refutation.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Ash'ari shows that if the Mu'tazila insist on full generality, then the negation would also deny perception by the heart, which they do not accept. Alternatively, if they limit the negation to physical eyes, their generality argument is broken.
This leads to the invalidation of their argument.
4
Consider the conclusion
Read the final lines where Ash'ari states that their argument is invalidated. Reflect on how a careful linguistic and logical analysis can disarm seemingly strong proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٨٥- باب في الرؤية :
احتجت المعتزلة في أن الله عز وجل لا يُرى بالأبصار بقوله تعالى: ﴿ لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٠٣] وقالوا فلما عطف الله عز وجل بقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ على قوله: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾، وكان قوله: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ على العموم أنه يدركها في الدنيا والآخرة، وأنه يراها في الدنيا والآخرة، كان قوله: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ دليلاً على أنه لا تراه الأبصار في الدنيا والآخرة، وكان في العموم قوله: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾؛ لأن أحد الكلامين معطوف على الآخر.

قيل لهم : فيجب إذا كان عموم القولين واحداً، وكانت الأبصار أبصار العيون وأبصار القلوب؛ لأن الله عزّ وجل قال: ﴿فَإِنَّهَا لَا تَعْمَى الْأَبْصَارُ وَلَكِن تَعْمَى الْقُلُوبُ الَّتِي فِي الصُّدُورِ﴾ [الحج: ٤٦]، وقال : ﴿أُولِي الْأَيْدِي وَالْأَبْصَارِ﴾ [ص:٤٥] فأراد أبصار القلوب وهي التي يفضل بها المؤمنون الكافرين.

ويقول أهل اللغة : فلان بصير بصناعته، يريدون بصر العلم، ويقولون: قد أبصرته بقلبي، كما يقولون قد أبصرته بعيني، فإذا كان البصر بصر العيون وبصر القلوب ثم أوجبوا علينا أن يكون قوله تعالى: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ في العموم كقوله: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ لأن أحد الكلامين معطوف على الآخر، وجب عليهم بحجتهم أن الله عز وجل لا يُدرك بأبصار العيون ولا بأبصار القلوب؛ لأن قوله: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ في العموم كقوله : ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ وإذا لم يكن عندهم هكذا فقد وجب أن يكون قوله: ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ أخص من قوله: ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ وانتقض احتجاجهم . وقيل لهم : إنكم زعمتم أنه لو كان قوله : ﴿لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ﴾ خاصاً في وقت دون وقت لكان قوله : ﴿وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ﴾ خاصاً في وقت دون
85 - Chapter on the Vision:
The Mu'tazila argued that Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, is not seen with the eyes by His saying, the Exalted: ﴾ Eyesight does not perceive Him, and He perceives eyesight ﴿ [al-An'am 6:103]. They said: When Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, joined His saying: ﴾ And He perceives eyesight ﴿ to His saying: ﴾ Eyesight does not perceive Him ﴿, and His saying: ﴾ And He perceives eyesight ﴿ is general that He perceives it in this world and the Hereafter, and that He sees it in this world and the Hereafter, then His saying: ﴾ Eyesight does not perceive Him ﴿ is evidence that eyesight does not see Him in this world and the Hereafter, and His saying: ﴾ And He perceives eyesight ﴿ is in generality, because one of the two statements is conjoined to the other.

It was said to them: Then it is necessary, if the generality of the two statements is one, and that 'eyesights' (absār) are the eyes of the eyes and the eyes of the hearts, because Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, said: ﴾ For indeed it is not the eyes that are blind, but blind are the hearts which are in the breasts ﴿ [al-Hajj 22:46], and He said: ﴾ Those possessed of hands and eyesight ﴿ [Saad 38:45], meaning the eyesight of the hearts, by which the believers are superior to the disbelievers.

And the people of language say: So-and-so is perceptive (basīr) in his craft, meaning the sight of knowledge, and they say: I have perceived him with my heart, just as they say: I have perceived him with my eye. So if 'sight' (al-basar) is the sight of the eyes and the sight of the hearts, and then they require of us that His saying, the Exalted: ﴾ Eyesight does not perceive Him ﴿ is in generality like His saying: ﴾ And He perceives eyesight ﴿, because one of the two statements is conjoined to the other, then it becomes necessary upon them by their own argument that Allah, the Might and Majestic, is not perceived by the eyes of the eyes nor by the eyes of the hearts, because His saying: ﴾ Eyesight does not perceive Him ﴿ is in generality like His saying: ﴾ And He perceives eyesight ﴿. And if it is not so according to them, then it becomes necessary that His saying: ﴾ Eyesight does not perceive Him ﴿ is more specific (akhas) than His saying: ﴾ And He perceives eyesight ﴿, and their argument is invalidated. And it was said to them: You claimed that if His saying: ﴾ Eyesight does not perceive Him ﴿ were specific in one time without another, then His saying: ﴾ And He perceives eyesight ﴿ would be specific in one time without another
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila argument against the vision of Allah (ru'ya). The author presents the Mu'tazila proof: they cite verse 6:103 'Eyesight does not perceive Him, and He perceives eyesight,' and argue that because the second part is general (all eyesight at all times), the first part is likewise general, negating any vision of Allah in both worlds. Ash'ari responds by analyzing the word 'absār' (eyesights). He notes that in Arabic, 'absār' can refer to physical eyes as well as the insight of the heart ('absār al-qulūb'), as shown in other Quranic verses (22:46, 38:45) and linguistic usage. If the Mu'tazila insist on the generality of both statements, then the negation in the first part would also negate perception by the heart's insight, which they do not accept. If they say the negation applies only to physical eyes, then the generality is broken, and their argument collapses. Ash'ari then points out that if the negation were specific to a particular time (e.g., this world), then the affirmation in the second part would also be specific, contradicting their claim. The argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum, showing that the Mu'tazila cannot consistently maintain their interpretation.

Key Terms
أبصار (absār)
إدراك (idrāk)
عموم (umūm)
خصوص (khuṣūṣ)
بصيرة (baṣīra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That verse 6:103 proves that Allah cannot be seen in the afterlife by anyone.
Correction: Ash'ari shows that the term 'absār' can refer to both physical eyes and inner insight. The Mu'tazila's generalization would also deny spiritual perception, which they accept, so their argument is self-contradictory.

Checkpoint
How does Ash'ari refute the Mu'tazila use of verse 6:103?
A) By saying the verse was abrogated.
B) By showing that 'absār' can mean both physical eyes and heart's insight, so the generality argument fails.
C) By denying the authenticity of the verse.
D) By claiming the verse refers only to this world.

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that the term 'absār' in the verse is ambiguous, and their insistence on generality leads to a contradiction they cannot accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to an interpretation of a religious text because it seemed logical, only to realize later that you ignored other possible meanings? How does this lesson challenge you to be more careful in your understanding of Allah's attributes?
?
The Mu'tazila denied the vision of Allah based on a narrow reading. Is there any area of your faith where you have limited Allah's attributes due to your own reasoning? What would it take for you to trust the broader linguistic and prophetic evidence?
?
Ash'ari points out that the heart has 'sight' too. How is your spiritual vision today? Are you using the insight of your heart to perceive Allah's signs, or is your heart blinded by worldly concerns? Commit to one specific action this week to sharpen your baṣīra.

The Mu'tazila's Argument and the Meaning of 'Absar'
Al-Ash'ari presents the Mu'tazila's proof and counters by redefining 'absar' as including both eyes and hearts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazila's argument by showing that 'absar' in the Quran includes both eyes and hearts.

Core Idea
The word 'absar' is not limited to physical eyes.

The Mu'tazila argue that since the verse says 'Vision does not perceive Him,' Allah cannot be seen. But Al-Ash'ari begins by examining the meaning of 'absar' in the Quran. He shows that it can refer to both physical sight and inner insight, which undermines their literal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's argument
Read the first few lines where the Mu'tazila present their proof from the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar'. Notice how they assume the word 'absar' refers only to physical eyes and that the negation is general for both worlds.
The Mu'tazila rely on the conjunction between the two clauses to claim generality.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'absar', 'ru'ya', and 'idrak'. Note how Al-Ash'ari will later redefine 'absar'.
3
See how Al-Ash'ari redefines 'absar'
Al-Ash'ari cites two Quranic verses and linguistic usage to show that 'absar' can mean both eyes and hearts. Read these examples carefully.
Verse 22:46 and 38:45 are used to show 'absar' meaning insight.
4
Understand the refutation
Al-Ash'ari argues that if 'absar' includes hearts, then the Mu'tazila's insistence on generality would also negate perception by hearts, which they do not accept. This inconsistency undermines their argument.
The refutation ends with 'wa-intaqada ihtijajuhum' (their argument is invalidated).

تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَرُ ) يوجب أن لا يدرك بها في الدنيا والآخرة، وليس ينفي ذلك أن نراه بقلوبنا، ونبصره بها، ولا ندركه بها . قيل لهم : فما أنكرتم أن يكون لا تدركه بأبصار العيون، ولا يوجب إذا لم ندركه بها أن لا نراه ونبصره بها فرؤيتنا له بالعيون وإبصارنا له بها ليس بإدراك لـه بها، كما أن إبصارنا له بالقلوب ورؤيتنا له بها ليس بإدراك له بها . فإن قالوا رؤية البصر هي إدراك البصر قيل لهم : ما الفرق بينكم وبين من قال : إن رؤية القلب وإبصاره هو إدراكه وإحاطته، فإذا كان علم القلب بالله عز وجل وإبصار القلب له ورؤيته إياه ليس بإحاطة ولا إدراك، فما أنكرتم أن تكون رؤية العيون وإبصارها الله عز وجل ليس بإحاطة ولا إدراك .
Chapter on Seeing (ar-Ru'ya). The Mu'tazila argued that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is not seen by eyes (al-absar) based on His statement: 'Vision does not perceive Him, and He perceives vision' (Surah al-An'am 6:103). They said: Since Allah, Mighty and Majestic, joined His statement 'and He perceives vision' to His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him', and His statement 'and He perceives vision' is general that He perceives it in this world and the Hereafter, and that He sees it in this world and the Hereafter, then His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is evidence that vision (al-absar) does not see Him in this world and the Hereafter, and His statement 'and He perceives vision' is general because one of the two statements is conjoined to the other. It was said to them: Then, if the generality of the two statements is one, and vision (al-absar) includes both the vision of eyes and the vision of hearts, because Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'For indeed they are not blind of the eyes, but the hearts within the breasts are blind' (Surah al-Hajj 22:46), and He said: '[He] of hands and vision' (Surah Sad 38:45), by which He meant the vision of hearts, by which the believers are distinguished from the disbelievers. And the people of language say: 'So-and-so is perspicacious (basir) in his craft', meaning the vision of knowledge, and they say: 'I have perceived him (abṣartuhu) with my heart', just as they say: 'I have seen him (abṣartuhu) with my eye.' So if vision (al-basar) is vision of eyes and vision of hearts, and then they required us that His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is general like His statement 'and He perceives vision' because one of the two statements is conjoined to the other, it would be necessary for them according to their argument that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is not perceived by vision of eyes nor by vision of hearts, because His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is in generality like His statement 'and He perceives vision'. And if it is not so according to them, then it would be necessary that His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is more specific than His statement 'and He perceives vision', and their argument would be invalidated. And it was said to them: You claimed that if His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' were specific to a time without a time, then His statement 'and He perceives vision' would be specific to a time without a time, and His statement 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Surah ash-Shura 42:11), and His statement 'Neither slumber nor sleep overtakes Him' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:255), and His statement 'God does not wrong people at all' (Surah Yunus 10:44) would be [specific] to a time without a time. If you make His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' specific, then your argument would turn against you, and it would be said to you: If His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is specific and the specificity of these verses is not required, then why do you deny that His statement, Mighty and Majestic, 'Vision does not perceive Him' meant only in this world and not the Hereafter? Just as His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' meant some vision (absar) without others, and that does not necessitate the specification of these verses which you have used against us. If they say: His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' necessitates that He is not perceived by it in this world and the Hereafter, and that does not negate that we see Him with our hearts and perceive Him by them, but we do not perceive (nudrik) Him by them. It was said to them: Then why do you deny that He is not perceived by the eyes, and if we do not perceive (nudrik) Him by them, that does not necessitate that we do not see (nara) Him and perceive (nubṣiruhu) Him by them? For our seeing (ru'yatuna) of Him with the eyes and our perceiving (ibṣaruna) Him by them is not an act of perception (idrak) of Him by them, just as our perceiving (ibṣaruna) Him with hearts and our seeing (ru'yatuna) Him by them is not an act of perception (idrak) of Him by them. If they say: Seeing (ru'ya) of the eye is the perception (idrak) of the eye, it is said to them: What is the difference between you and one who says: Seeing (ru'ya) of the heart and its perception (ibṣar) is its grasping (idrak) and encompassing (ihata)? For if the knowledge of the heart of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, and the heart's perception (ibṣar) of Him and its seeing (ru'ya) of Him is not encompassing (ihata) nor perception (idrak), then why do you deny that seeing (ru'ya) of the eyes and their perception (ibṣar) of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is not encompassing (ihata) nor perception (idrak)?
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's argument against the possibility of seeing Allah (ar-ru'ya) based on the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (Vision does not perceive Him). Al-Ash'ari presents the Mu'tazila's proof: they argue that because the verse says 'does not perceive Him' and is conjoined with 'and He perceives vision' which is general for both worlds, the negation is also general for both worlds. Al-Ash'ari then refutes this by first pointing out that 'absar' in the Quran can refer to both eyes and hearts, citing verses where 'absar' means insight. He argues that if the Mu'tazila insist on the general application of the negation, then it would also negate perception by hearts, which they do not hold. Therefore, their argument is inconsistent. He further argues that if they claim the negation is specific (to eyes or to this world), then their own argument collapses. The Mu'tazila then shift to argue that the negation of idrak does not negate ru'ya with hearts. Al-Ash'ari responds that if ru'ya with hearts is not idrak, then ru'ya with eyes can also be not idrak. He challenges the Mu'tazila's claim that ru'ya of the eye is necessarily idrak, pointing out that ru'ya of the heart is not idrak or ihata, so ru'ya of the eyes need not be either. The key distinction is between ru'ya (seeing) and idrak (perception/grasping). The Mu'tazila conflate them; Al-Ash'ari separates them, allowing for seeing Allah without encompassing Him.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila thought 'absar' refers only to physical eyes in the verse.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari demonstrates that 'absar' in the Quran can also refer to hearts or inner sight, so the verse does not necessarily deny seeing with physical eyes.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Ash'ari, what does the word 'absar' (plural of basar) include based on Quranic usage?
A) Only the physical eyes of humans
B) Both the eyes and the hearts
C) Only the hearts of believers
D) The eyes of angels and humans

Key Takeaway
'Absar' in the Quran can mean both physical eyes and inner hearts, so the verse does not automatically negate seeing Allah with the eyes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran, do you assume your understanding is complete, or do you, like Al-Ash'ari, consider that words may carry deeper meanings that challenge your initial interpretation? How willing are you to let the Quran expand your understanding of Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila limited their concept of 'seeing' to physical eyes. In your own spiritual life, have you limited your perception of Allah to what your senses or intellect can grasp, ignoring the deeper vision of the heart? What would it mean for you to truly 'see' with your heart?
?
Al-Ash'ari's refutation relies on careful attention to language and logic. How much time do you spend reflecting on the exact words of the Quran and their implications for your faith, rather than relying on surface-level readings? Will you commit to studying a verse this week with the same precision?

The Generality vs. Specificity Dilemma
Al-Ash'ari presents a dilemma: if the negation is general, it conflicts with belief in heart-vision; if specific, the Mu'tazila's argument fails.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's argument that the Mu'tazila face a dilemma: if they treat the negation as general, it leads to an absurdity; if they treat it as specific, their argument collapses.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila cannot have it both ways.

Al-Ash'ari continues his refutation by pointing out a logical inconsistency in the Mu'tazila's position. If they insist that the negation in the verse is general for all times and all kinds of 'absar', then they must also accept that Allah is not perceived by hearts. If they say it is specific to certain times or kinds, then their own argument against seeing Allah in the afterlife fails. This dilemma shows the weakness of their proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditional argument
Al-Ash'ari presents a conditional: if the Mu'tazila claim the negation is specific to a time, then the positive clause would also be specific. This is based on their own reasoning about conjunction.
The Mu'tazila argued that because the two clauses are conjoined, they must have the same scope.
2
See the parallel verses
Al-Ash'ari cites other verses like 'There is nothing like unto Him' and 'Neither slumber nor sleep overtakes Him' and 'God does not wrong people at all' to show that if the Mu'tazila apply specificity to the vision verse, they would have to apply it to these verses as well, which is absurd.
These verses are also general in scope, but the Mu'tazila would not accept them as specific to certain times.
3
Understand the conclusion
The Mu'tazila cannot consistently claim the negation is specific without undermining their own position. Their argument either leads to an absurdity (if general) or self-destructs (if specific).
Al-Ash'ari says: 'If you make it specific, your argument turns against you.'

Chapter on Seeing (ar-Ru'ya). The Mu'tazila argued that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is not seen by eyes (al-absar) based on His statement: 'Vision does not perceive Him, and He perceives vision' (Surah al-An'am 6:103). They said: Since Allah, Mighty and Majestic, joined His statement 'and He perceives vision' to His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him', and His statement 'and He perceives vision' is general that He perceives it in this world and the Hereafter, and that He sees it in this world and the Hereafter, then His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is evidence that vision (al-absar) does not see Him in this world and the Hereafter, and His statement 'and He perceives vision' is general because one of the two statements is conjoined to the other. It was said to them: Then, if the generality of the two statements is one, and vision (al-absar) includes both the vision of eyes and the vision of hearts, because Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'For indeed they are not blind of the eyes, but the hearts within the breasts are blind' (Surah al-Hajj 22:46), and He said: '[He] of hands and vision' (Surah Sad 38:45), by which He meant the vision of hearts, by which the believers are distinguished from the disbelievers. And the people of language say: 'So-and-so is perspicacious (basir) in his craft', meaning the vision of knowledge, and they say: 'I have perceived him (abṣartuhu) with my heart', just as they say: 'I have seen him (abṣartuhu) with my eye.' So if vision (al-basar) is vision of eyes and vision of hearts, and then they required us that His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is general like His statement 'and He perceives vision' because one of the two statements is conjoined to the other, it would be necessary for them according to their argument that Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is not perceived by vision of eyes nor by vision of hearts, because His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is in generality like His statement 'and He perceives vision'. And if it is not so according to them, then it would be necessary that His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is more specific than His statement 'and He perceives vision', and their argument would be invalidated. And it was said to them: You claimed that if His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' were specific to a time without a time, then His statement 'and He perceives vision' would be specific to a time without a time, and His statement 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Surah ash-Shura 42:11), and His statement 'Neither slumber nor sleep overtakes Him' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:255), and His statement 'God does not wrong people at all' (Surah Yunus 10:44) would be [specific] to a time without a time. If you make His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' specific, then your argument would turn against you, and it would be said to you: If His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' is specific and the specificity of these verses is not required, then why do you deny that His statement, Mighty and Majestic, 'Vision does not perceive Him' meant only in this world and not the Hereafter? Just as His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' meant some vision (absar) without others, and that does not necessitate the specification of these verses which you have used against us. If they say: His statement 'Vision does not perceive Him' necessitates that He is not perceived by it in this world and the Hereafter, and that does not negate that we see Him with our hearts and perceive Him by them, but we do not perceive (nudrik) Him by them. It was said to them: Then why do you deny that He is not perceived by the eyes, and if we do not perceive (nudrik) Him by them, that does not necessitate that we do not see (nara) Him and perceive (nubṣiruhu) Him by them? For our seeing (ru'yatuna) of Him with the eyes and our perceiving (ibṣaruna) Him by them is not an act of perception (idrak) of Him by them, just as our perceiving (ibṣaruna) Him with hearts and our seeing (ru'yatuna) Him by them is not an act of perception (idrak) of Him by them. If they say: Seeing (ru'ya) of the eye is the perception (idrak) of the eye, it is said to them: What is the difference between you and one who says: Seeing (ru'ya) of the heart and its perception (ibṣar) is its grasping (idrak) and encompassing (ihata)? For if the knowledge of the heart of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, and the heart's perception (ibṣar) of Him and its seeing (ru'ya) of Him is not encompassing (ihata) nor perception (idrak), then why do you deny that seeing (ru'ya) of the eyes and their perception (ibṣar) of Allah, Mighty and Majestic, is not encompassing (ihata) nor perception (idrak)?
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's argument against the possibility of seeing Allah (ar-ru'ya) based on the verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' (Vision does not perceive Him). Al-Ash'ari presents the Mu'tazila's proof: they argue that because the verse says 'does not perceive Him' and is conjoined with 'and He perceives vision' which is general for both worlds, the negation is also general for both worlds. Al-Ash'ari then refutes this by first pointing out that 'absar' in the Quran can refer to both eyes and hearts, citing verses where 'absar' means insight. He argues that if the Mu'tazila insist on the general application of the negation, then it would also negate perception by hearts, which they do not hold. Therefore, their argument is inconsistent. He further argues that if they claim the negation is specific (to eyes or to this world), then their own argument collapses. The Mu'tazila then shift to argue that the negation of idrak does not negate ru'ya with hearts. Al-Ash'ari responds that if ru'ya with hearts is not idrak, then ru'ya with eyes can also be not idrak. He challenges the Mu'tazila's claim that ru'ya of the eye is necessarily idrak, pointing out that ru'ya of the heart is not idrak or ihata, so ru'ya of the eyes need not be either. The key distinction is between ru'ya (seeing) and idrak (perception/grasping). The Mu'tazila conflate them; Al-Ash'ari separates them, allowing for seeing Allah without encompassing Him.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila thought they could claim the negation is general for both worlds but specific in terms of kind (only eyes).
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that consistency demands that if it is general in time, it must be general in kind as well, which they reject.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Ash'ari argue would happen if the Mu'tazila claim the negation in the verse is specific to a certain time?
A) The positive clause would also be specific, and other general verses would have to be specific too.
B) The verse would become meaningless.
C) The Mu'tazila's argument would become even stronger.
D) The verse would only apply to the disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila cannot consistently argue for a general negation without also denying perception by hearts, nor can they argue for a specific negation without undermining their own position.

?
When you engage with difficult theological questions, do you ever find yourself wanting to apply one rule to verses you like and a different rule to verses you dislike? How does Al-Ash'ari's challenge to the Mu'tazila expose that tendency in your own heart? Will you commit to consistent interpretation, even when it goes against your preference?
?
Imagine you were in a debate and your argument was turned back on you as Al-Ash'ari did here. Would you have the humility to admit your inconsistency, or would you double down? What does your answer reveal about your attachment to being right over submitting to the truth of the Quran?

Distinguishing Ru'ya from Idrak
Al-Ash'ari refutes the Mu'tazila's final objection by distinguishing ru'ya from idrak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Ash'ari's key distinction between seeing (ru'ya) and perceiving (idrak), and how this allows for seeing Allah without encompassing Him.

Core Idea
Seeing does not require full comprehension.

The Mu'tazila try to salvage their argument by claiming that the verse only negates 'idrak' (perception), not 'ru'ya' (seeing), but only for the heart. Al-Ash'ari turns this against them: if the heart can see Allah without idrak, then the eye can also see without idrak. The distinction between ru'ya and idrak is crucial: idrak implies encompassing or fully grasping, while ru'ya is mere sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's new claim
The Mu'tazila say that the negation of idrak does not negate ru'ya with hearts. They admit that we can see Allah with our hearts without perceiving (idrak) Him.
This is a concession that Al-Ash'ari will exploit.
2
See Al-Ash'ari's response
Al-Ash'ari asks: why can't the same be true for the eyes? If seeing with the heart is not idrak, then seeing with the eyes might also be not idrak.
The key is that 'idrak' means full comprehension or encompassment, not mere sight.
3
Understand the final analogy
Al-Ash'ari uses the analogy of the heart: if the heart's knowledge and seeing of Allah is not idrak or ihata (encompassing), then the eye's seeing can also be without idrak. The Mu'tazila have no basis to claim that ru'ya of the eye necessarily equals idrak.
The verse 'La tudrikuhu al-absar' thus negates idrak, not ru'ya.
